THE FI-A.TXTO TRADE.
Messrs. Beale & Co.’s Great Enterprise.
rpIIERE can be no qu s killing the fact that the Piano
1 Trade of t.hcise Colonies has undergone a complete
revolution during the last five years ; and to that desirable
change certainly no firm has contributed more than
Messrs. Beale and Company. During the time high prices
continued the trade was held hy a few, and hundreds of
homes now rendered attractive by the glorious influence
of music scarce could dream of becoming the ultimate
owners of the instrument which has done so much to
cheer and to refine our modern domestic life.
When Beale and Company commenced business they
had quietly gauged this condition of affairs in Australia,
and they began operations on a .plan which could alone
work out successively in a mammoth undertaking such as
they proposed. With the Australian selling branch of
their enterprise they resolved to combine the manufacture
of Pianos in Germany—the centre of the world's trade for
that instrument. But the experts sent out here for the
purpose of fully determinin'.: what was best fitted for
colonial requirements reported that it was essential to
have a special make of piano to suit our climate—an in-
strument as different as those made for Europe as Aus-
tralia is different from the Old World. The result of this
was the production of the staunchest pianoforte of
modern times—the iron-framed “ Hapsburg.” The in-
strument is regarded by the most capable experts of
Europe as the Production of the Age, combining as
it does every feature of the highest class instrument with
the maximum of strength and durability, and it was placed
on the market at prices much under what other firms and
manufacturers had previously demanded. Although it
took almost endless trouble and long, patient labour to
produce the design of the “ Inimitable Hapsburg,” yet once
produced the demand giew so rapidly, and has become so
great, that Hie enterprising originators are able to out-
distance all competitors for both quality and price. The
peculiar construction of the iron frame, the excellence
of the wires, and the unique movements have, with all
other factors, contributed to this splendid success. No
other instrument, possesses to the same degree such rich
full, clear, hell-like tones, so noticeable in the “Haps-
burg,” especially in the higher grades, while strength,
solidity, and imperviousness to climatic influences have
been so well looked to that whether on t.ie coldest n eak-
e'st spots of New- Zealand or our own Monaro Plains, or in
the hottest of our Western and Northern areas, this
wonderful pianoforte not only maintains, but improves its
tone and consequent value even after years of use. And
it must continue so. The genius and experience of the
mechanical world in this branch of art are embodied i ■
this one instrument - the inimitable “ Hapsburg:”- a d
the sales to-day give ample evidence of the repute in whi h
it is held. Beale & Company have placed the
“Hapsburg” with thousands of families in Australia.
Homes in the far-distant back blocks, and homes in the
city, are not only enlivened hut are made purer and better
through the influence of its music. The firm resolved (o
do a large trade by selling these superior instru-
ments at prices and on terms demanded foV the
lower grades, and success lias crowned their efforts.
They do by far the largest business, and hold the
largest stocks in the colonies. They give their best
efforts to suit the circumstances and requirements of
every customer, and they supply for cash or on an easy
system of Time-payment which they have made pecu'iarly
their own. The correspondence department is of itself a
huge undertaking; every mail brings its burden of letters.
From the great landowner, from the rich squatter, and
the astute merchant they come. And they came too
from clergymen, from doctors and solicitors, front civil
servants, and from the struggling selector, the tradesman,
the workingman ; for fathers and mothers of every grade
and class now recognise the influence which music exerts
for good in the life of the home. And every writer re-
ceives courteous attention and immediate response.
Hapsburg Double Iron-framed Pianos
Possess the following Advantages —
-Ist. —The wires are of a very high quality and in-
tensely sensitive ; their vibrations, reverberated
by a sounding board of special construction, are
clear and bell-like, instead of the muffled, tin-
kettly, or metallic sounds so common in cheap
Pianos.
2nd.—Being double oblique the strings are of a
greater length and fuller tone than in the old
straight-strung Pianos.
3rd. —The action is perfect check repeater, and is
made of pencil cedar, an expensive material,
and best for resisting climatic changes.
4th.— All tl re Hapsburar Pianos are Trichord.
sth.—They are all seven Octaves.
6th.—They have consolidated Iron Frames of
great strength, thus resisting the rapid muta-
tions of a tropical climate, whereby tuning is
only required at long intervals.
7th. —They oppose obstacles to the inroads of cock-
roaches, silver-fish, mice, &c. They have wire
gauze backs which cannot be eaten by vermin,
and other unique improvements devised for
similiar ends.
Bth. —The Felts are Clinched on the Hammers,
instead of being fastened only by glue, as is the
case with nearly every Piano except the Haps-
burg.
9th.—They have brass or nickel-faced Wrest-
planks, instead of the old-fashioned perishable
style.
10th.—Rust is guarded against, wherever possible,
by the substitution of bronze for other metals.
11th.—The outer cases are handsome in design,
and of durable beautiful woods; they can be
had in walnut, or in walnut and gold, or in
ebony and gold.
12th.—No matter in what part of the colony you
live, we will supply you with a Hapsburg Piano
upon easy terms of payment. No charge will be
made for packing or for packing cases.
Hapsburg Pianos. Haydn Organs.
Photographs and Price-lists by post or from our
own travellers everywhere.

This admirable little treatise is specially written to
show a method of counteracting the evil effects of indis-

Western Australia is a much richer colony than is
generally supposed as regards minerals, agriculture and
pastoralisin.— Sir Samuel Griffith.
Professor Brown, of Victoria, who made a thorough
examination of the south-western portion of Western
Australia, states that not the other colonies only, but
even the people of Western Australia evident ly do not
know how much there is waiting for development, lie
saw thousands of acres in contiguous blocks of fine choco-
late lands, light clay loams, and peaty soils. He thinks
the important features of the distiiot are its grand climate
and regular rainfall.
“ History repeals itself,” and as surely as Sydney and
Melbourne have made lightning-like progress, so will
PERTH,
the Capital of West Australia.

SATURDAY. JUNE 7, 1890.
The Hours of Toil.
The great want of the European labourer
is leisure. Under existing conditions that
leisure will never come. In England his
work averages eleven hours daily : in Fiance
and Germany twelve : in other countries
fifteen hours are his quota. To him cessation
of work, if not for hard-earned rest, is for
idleness ; rarely does it mean leisure. Idle-
ness—that is, time subtracted from necessary
employment—is ruin to the labourer and
danger to the State, while leisure, on the
contrary, being time not essential to the
procuring of necessaries, is a boon to the
individual and the mainspring of social pro-
gress. In Europe, as we have said, it is
either all work or all idleness, and the evils
of idleness are so obvious as to attract the
attention of even baby-thinkers.
* # •
Leisure as a condition has already been de-
scribed, as an instrument it may be defined
as the parent of social opportunity. If
further definition be needed, social oppor-
tunity may be taken to mean the absence of
restraints by which the capacity to acquire
wants or to develope the mind is repressed.
Buckle says “ Whatever the moral and in-
tellectual progress of man may be. it resolves
itself not into a progress of natural capacity,
but into a progress of opportunity—that is,
an improvement in the circumstances under
which that capacity, after birth, comes into
play." All progress is the fruit of oppor-
tunity. The opportunity of satisfying a
desire begets the desire, and Bastiat, in
common with other writers, shows that by
continuous satisfaction a desire becomes a
taste, a taste a want, and a want an imperi-
ous need that will not be denied. Further
than this, the satisfaction of wants generates
other desires, which in their turn become
wants as opportuniiies of gratification present
themselves, so that the larger the number of
wants which we develope, the larger also the
number we may be likely to acquire.
• # •
This is not a matter of metaphysical induc-
tion, but rather of common notoriety. It. is
(rue equally "f bodily wants as of mental
of nations as of individuals ; it is the explana-
tion of all progress, and of the continuous
advancement which the human race always
makes when in a position to freely exercise
legitimate desires. In the individual its
operation is very easily traced. The ill-fed
European labourer, when he makes Australia
his home, speedily acquires a taste for Aus-
tralian fare, which developes rapidly into a
need ; with a full stomach come desires for
liberty, and he who in Europe was a syco-
phant of lordlings becomes in Australia a
democrat. There is a curve known to
mathematicians as the cissoid, which begins
with scarcely a perceptible rise from the
horizontal, continuing this parallelism for a
while ; it presently shows a gap which in-
creases so perceptibly that the curve forsakes
the horizontal for a rapidly-ascending posi-
tion. Such is the path of progress which, as
we have said, is the creature of opportunity.
How, then, is this opportunity to be created?
* * #
To our mind the chief and most sure way
is by securing shorter hours. In vain are
there built and furnished Museums, ArtGal-
laries, and Libraries, if those for whom they
are intended do not have opportunity for
their enjoyment. With the leisure that short
hours afford, would come, gradually, to be
sure, but in all certainty, the appreciation of
Art and all that ministers to the finer feelings.
r Jhe development of taste would bring about
a demand for material comforts, and a
stronger appreciation of the things which
humanize the common lot. The demand and
the opportunity for its gratification would
make these comforts necessaries, and raise
ihe standard of living which 1 lie worker
would deem to be a necessary collocation of
his toil.’ It may be argued that the demand
might be never so strong, while the qualifica-
tion would be as far off as ever—that a man’s
sense of his own importance, may make him
desire wholesome and sufficient food for him-
self and his family, respectable clothes and
comfortable lodging, but his ability to pro-
vide these things may be entirely wanting.
This is not the case, as we shall show.
# # #
That the united labour of any country is
capable of producing sufficient to enable its
people to enjoy a larger share of products
than they now obtain, is rarely denied, nor
does there appear anything in the nature of
things to prevent such a distribution taking
place. The curse of our present system is
that while more than sufficient can be pro-
duced, less than sufficient reaches the
worker. It is computed that an equal dis-
tribution of what the labour of any country
can produce in any year would raise the
material condition of the labouring classes to
that now enjoyed by the so-called middle-
classes. Such being the case, how is it that
the life of the labourer is such a wretched
one ? This was the question that troubled
Wat Tyler and his men when they de-
manded—
When Adam delved and Eve span
IVho was then the gentleman ?
Wat and his peasants saw two things clearly
enough—they did the work and others
reaped the gain ; nor do we see that matters
have been mended very much since his day.
# # #
Let us cease from speculation awhile and
look at the matter through the medium of a
few figures relating to one of the Australias.
Take N.S. Wales: During the last three years
in that province 40 persons departed from this
vale of tears leaving behind them £4.227,00*);
in the same period there died 42,640 otheis
whose accumulations amounted to£8,042,000;
and of these latter. 37,728 had nothing to
leave ' The plain English of this is that
less than one-tenth per cent, of the popula-
tion own more than one-third the wealth of
the community, while 11 per cent, own the
other two-thirds, and the remaining 80 per
cent, have, of course, nothing. This is de-
lightful, reminding us so much of dear old
t ugland. It is obvious from the evidence of
our own eyes, even if we had not ample
proof from statistics that the distribution of
products is just, about as uneven as it could
well be. Let us turn it round another way.
It has been ascertained that the average
income of the Australian people is £42 per
head. Allowing 4| persons to a family the
average income of each would be £l7B.
That the average worker has more than £lOB
a year we very much doubt, so that the
worker produces from the natural resources
of the country three-fourths more than he
gets back for his support ! The process by
which the hulk of his earnings arc abstracted
Karl Marx calls exploitation. Which
raises a question we did not propose to dis-
cuss when we started this article.
# # #
To resume : It may be assumed as proven
that the sum total of the products of human
toil, if evenly distributed, would afford a
much larger share of material comforts to the
people producing them than they now enjoy.
Also that a labourer’s share of the product is
ordinarily sufficient to meet the demands of
the [least advanced] members of his class.
That this standard is below the equivalent of
the average production of the labourer.
That the most efficient means of raising the
standard of comfort is by affording the
labourer social opportunity, and this latter is
synonymous with leisure. That for the
labourer leisure means shorter hours. It
remains to be seen that shorter hours will
not only afford leisure, but abolish its worst
foe, idleness.
# * *
Let us assume t he average day s labour in
Europe I" be twelve boms If the eight
hour rule were general to compass the work
now done, would need 33 per cent, more
hands than at present find employment. On
an average about 7 to 10 per cent, of the
labouring class arc always out of employment,
8 per cent, are paupers maintained by the
State, and of the workers in employment 10
per cent, are women who should be at
domestic duties, and 5 to 8 per cent, are
children of tender years. It is clear, there-
fore, that if the hours were shortened com-
pulsory idleness would be abolished, able-
bodied pauperism would have no cause to
exist, children of tender years could be
released from toil, and women who should
be at home, would be set free. The advan-
tages of short hours are therefore that all
would find employment and all would have
leisure, which we have demonstrated is the
parent of all progress. The objections to the
scheme are these. The sudden introduction
of short hours would upset trade—to this we
say let it be not sudden but general. Again, if
eight hoursbegood, why not four, or even two?
To this we would say, if we were not serious,
why not twenty instead of twelve, why,
indeed, not twenty-three ? We have shown
what eight hours would do for Europe, viz.,
without decreasing production—abolish the
things that are now, after the military
system, its chief curse ; but no one claims
any special virtue in tne number eight. We
maintain, however, there is an economic
number of hours constitut ng a day’s work,
to wit, that number in which the average
labourer can produce what is necessary for
his subsistence and the maintenance of the
government under which he and his fellows
choose to live. We see no difficulty in ascer-
taining what this number is. It may, indeed,
differ in various countries, but it is always
the quotient, when the dividend is the pro-
duction required to maintain the whole body
of labourers and the government of the
country for one day, and the divisor is the
production of the average labourer in one
hour.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Too Monotonous.
“ Peuishfd through becoming too monotonous ”
will be the inscription over the dust of the
present Victorian Government when the country
at last gets rid of that legislative heirloom. The
Gii.liks Administration ha distinctly seen its beet
days, and the province is gradually falling away
from its allegiance on tile ground that the old in-
stitution makes it fed exhausted. There is a
weariness in the ghastly 'espectability of the
Coalition Cabinet—an awful » oneness in its high
moral attitude ; it is the political embodiment of
the good young man who didn't die, and a certain
used-up feeling which prevails among the consti-
tuencies is the first sign o' a re volt against an era of
legislative melancholy, 'i here is no very tangible
complaint to make against the lion. Duncan and
his colleagues—only the Victorians, like the
Israelites of old, have got tired of hash. The
Government, it is true, has grovelled a good deal,
but then the Opposition has grovelled with equal
vigour and enthusiasm. It has muddled up the
railway administration to a considerable extent,
till at last the cobwebs have begun to accumulate
in the special express train, and the traveller who
is in ha-tc prefers to hire a wheelbarrow propelled
by a clippie for the sake of the extra velocity thus
acquired; but judging from the mental calibre of
the men on the other side of the House, they will
probably inaugurate a system under which a fly
in a glue-pot will race against the petrified
iron horse, and leave that protracted fossil
hopelessly behind. It has muddled the
finances to some slight extent, so that an
expected surplus of a. million and three-quarters
turned out to be in actual fact merely a surplus of
To*M,0(10, but then there is no financial genius in
Mr. Mvnko’s following who is likely to prove a
better shot, and whereas the piesent Treasurer
managed at least to cripple his surplus at long
range, the chances are that any possible successor
would miss it altogether. All the ostensible
reasons put forth to account for the growing
public dissatisfaction are too thin ; the real grief
is that the Cabinet has got to be monotonous.
The Premier’s mirthless smile makes the country
wish it were dead. His mild, unobtrusive aspect
makes the country half-inclined to wish he were
dead, too. The amendment wh'ch the country
wants to move is to the effect that the Ministry is
too ordinary and solemn and commonplace to be
endured much longer—that it is too plain and too
woefully respectable. Nobody ever raises a bright
and refreshing row in the Victorian Assembly
now. Nobody swears in the pleasant, affable way
which creates an agreeable oasis in the great
sandy desert of debate in the other colonies. The
average New South Wales statesman can offer up
cuss language for seventy-five minutes without
repeating the same profanity twice, but the most
powerful expressions habitually used by the Vic-
torian Ministry are mild compared to those in an
ordinary hymn. When the Government does get
into a rage its indignation is like unto the indig-
nation of the clam or the unobtrusive whelk, and
its fury is akin to the fury of the angry bun at a
Sunday school spread. It wears a charitable
smile which gets tedious after long acquaintance.
Tt is solid and ponderous after the manner of
Uncle Pumiu.echook. It has a general expression
which seems to suggest that it takes care of its
aged grandfather, and is the sole supjxirt of a
venerated aunt and a family of ten orphans, all of
them of the most respectable habits. It looks as
if it had a small sum of money deposited in a very
secure bank somewhere as a provision for a rainy
day, and there is an ineffable something about it
which states distinctly that its mother died when
it was very young, and that it habitually goes
round and collects a flower off her. grave. There
is an aspect of humble remonstrance in its eye
which explains that it feels bad at the shortness of
the average ballet-girl’s attire, and even looking
at it from behind anyone can see that a person who
attempted to read an immoral poem to it would be
immediately given into custody, while an effort to
trade off an improper photograph would probably
be accompanied by fatal results to the vendor.
It looks as if it had been opened with prayer,
whereas the ordinary New South Wales Cabinet
looks as if it had been opened with drink, and
closed with blasphemy and an axe. Anybody
except a lunatic can see at the first glance that it
was born of poor but honest parents, and that
they left it to be brought up “ by hand ” by
an estimable female relative. It is the sort of
Government which might reasonably be expected
to suffer front cold feet, and to wear goloshes in
consequence, and it looks as if it was partial to
cats. It frequently wears a shocked expression,
and seems as if it were feeling hurt. Somebody
connected with it offered a second-hand humorous
observation away back in the year 1862, and that
uncalled-for levity has preyed on its mind ever
since. If it came across a low, depraved heathen
person who had nothing on except a ring stuck
through his nose it would give vent to a shriek and
leave the spot with great velocity, but it would
afterwards transmit to him through the post a
solemn tract which he would be unable to under-
stand. If it met him a second time it would
speak a few words of Christian remonstrance, and
urge him to be industrious and go to church regu-
larly. If it accidentally came out without its
umbrella, and anybody drew its attention to the
defect, it would utter the weird howl of a maniac
who had made an exhibition of himself, and send
in its resignation. The first impression which it
leaves on the mind of a stranger is that it is
striving to save up enough to buy a small annuity
for its old age, and is not getting along very suc-
cessfully in the attempt ; the second is that it is
longing to go out and do good. On closer inspec-
tion the observer becomes convinced that it
would sternly admonish any small boy whom
it found swearing on Sunday, and would
point out to him where he is likely to
go to when he dies, and ho can also faintly
picture the language that small bov would be
likely to use in reply, it is the sort of Cabinet
whi h alwavs wears its coat tightly buttoned up,
an 1 is careful not to sit on the tails ot that gar-
ment ; and as a natural consequence of these and
a number of other virtues the Gillies Administra-
tion is getting to be too monotonous. If it would
even go out and commit one refreshing sin that
had some originality about it the country would
feel better, but it never does ; it perpetrates some
old, respectable, monotonous, second-hand sin now
and then, and that is all. And, after all, the
chances are that, if it did take to the flowery paths
of dissipation, it would get drunk so respectably
that the public would not leel at all relieved ; and
if it left suddenly with a 1 -dy who didn’t belong
to it, it won d elope in such a moral and inexcep-
tion able manner that ordinary men who had never
eloped at all would feel bad and depraved by com-
parison. Therefore, the Coalition Government
begins to show signs of being in the sere and
yellow leaf, and when it dies the inscription above
its remains will set forth the fact that it perished
because its plain respectability had made upwards
of a million people leel exhausted for a long time,
till at last they couldn’t stand it any longer.
The Dominant Power in Western
Australia.
As illustration is better than theory we reprint
below portion of a letter written in all seriousness
by “ An Old Conservative ” to the West Austra-
lian of Bth May
For, sir, if we look back upon the history of even the
last decade of years what do we find? Do we find that
even apart froi'p politics we have benefited so largely from
the advent of these new-cotneis? Can they point to any-
thing which, as compared with its state ten years back, is
the better for their presence in the colony? Ten years
ago we were progressing quietly hut steadily. We did
not travel by leaps and hounds. Everything went on as
smoothly as could he and everybody was happy, fairly
prosperous, and well-to-do. Then we began to he
troubled with adventurers. At first they came
singly, or by twos and threes ; afterwards they
poured in in shoals. All manner of schemes were
projected by them, ostensibly for the general good, in
reality, many of them, to serve ends which were infinitely
more selfish than even the selfishness of wicked landed
conservatism, and were certainly nefarious. Some of the
arrivals, we know by their acts since, were most desirable
men, and we are glad they reaped their reward. We are
likewise glad that some of the others have also reaped
their reward. Some of the latter have levanted rich in
pocket, and poorer, if possible, in reputation than when
they arrived. Others have fled as penniless as they came,
only because justice was at their heels. Some, too,
have reluctantly gone into enforced retirement. It' is
gentlemen of this kind wc are determined to keep out of
Parliament, hence the property qualification. No doubt,
in doing so, we shall keep out men of brains, and even
honest men ; hut the colony has gone on so well under
the auspices ot “property,” and till a few years ago
everyone was so contented, that we can well afford to
retain the clause. If it were simply a question of country
it is possible the Conservatives would give way ; but it is
merely one of loaves and fishes to the “progressive”
partv.' It is pleasing to read of “certain persons ” acting
“as’ little Providences over their own and their
families’ fortunes,” and it will he more pleasing to
“Demos” and his friends to learn that we intend
to continue using our family influence, and ultim-
ately man the whole civil service with our -sous and
our nephews.- And, indeed, rather than “progress”
in the way we are asked to, the old Conservatives would,
almost to a man, prefer to go hack to the old days of
Crown Colony Government. So convinced am 1 that the
colony would he better off without those gentlemen who
compose the “progressive” party, that I would gladly
subscribe my £1 to a fund—if such were raised - to charter
a vessel and convey them far from the shores of a country
where they are not wanted.—Yours, &c.,
Ax Old Conservative.
By Cable.
The London Economist ment ions in the stertorous
way peculiar to that journal that it thinks those
branch-managers of the Bank of New Zealand
who have “been loyal to the institution ’’ should
keep their billets, and those who haven’t been
loyal should be sacked, and the intelligent corres-
pondent goes to the expense of cabling out that
flat and apoplectic platitude as a piece of valuable
advice. Why, of course, any manager who hasn’t
been “ loyal ” will be sacked. Aren’t they always
sacked ? Why doesn’t the correspondent telegraph
out that the STANDARD is inclined to consider
that any high official in the bank who habitually
chases a large depositor down the street with a
boomerang ought to be suspended, and that many
important financial authorities are inclined to
think that the practice of keeping the coals in the
safe and stowing the bank’s securities out in the
hen-house is contrary to the. best interests of the
institution ? Also, we would be glad to know by
cable, regardless of expense, if the Times is of
opinion that an accountant who drives the Board’s
hat down over its ears by banging that
august body on the head when it isn’t look-
ing should be refused a rise of salary, or if
the Statist holds that an officer suffering from
incurable lunacy ought to have a minute made
about him by the directors? Further, we want
the intelligent correspondent to cable whether it
is considered advisable by leading English experts
that the cash should be short when the auditor
comes round, and whether the practice of having
aboriginal bookkeepers ought not to be dis-
couraged? In fact, we want all sorts of useless
information on the subject of banking, or any
other subject, always provided that it is of exclu-
sively British origin. If the. London Economist
is of opinion that it is unhealthy to be in front of a
cannon when it goes off, or that long-continued
hanging is generally fatal, or that there are mile-
stones on the Dover-road, or that a verb ought to
agree with its nominative provided it can be done
without bursting the nominative and making the
verb feel exhausted, we wish to be made aware of
the fact right off. What is the use of England
either, for that matter, if we are unable to profit
by the learning and accumulated culture which
she diffuses around her when she states through
the medium of the press that a British prince
looked with a weary, unintelligent, far-away
expression at a sample of Australian butter,
and was graciously pleased to notice that there
was a fly in it ? What is the use of anything at all,
in fact, under such circumstances* 'We feel com-
pelled to catch hold of some heavy article of
furniture to prevent onr emotion lifting us off
our feet as we think of what this country would
come to if it hadn’t these solid truths to keep it in
a state of intellectual activity. Supposing the
cable breaks some day, and we are cut off from
these accustomed sources of inspiration, and-the
London correspondent is fainting at the pump at


the other end of the crippled wire in his vain
efforts to telegraph out that some choice financial
noodle thinks that any bank-manager located at
Sydney, near Dunedin, in the country of Victoria
and province of Queensland, who has done some-
thing he ought not to have done, should have
something done to him, in a manner of speaking,
and ought to be looked upon more or less with an
unfavourable eye in a mitigated sort of way, and
consequently that valuable fact can’t get through
in time for us to profit by it ! Where will Austra-
lia be under these disastrous conditions? In
ruins, most probably, and the inscription above
its dust will set forth that it died for want of in-
formation, and perished miserably through its
own dense colonial ignorance.
+
An Old Legal Relic.
Some years ago a feeble attempt was made in New
Zealand to sort out the chaos of old, spavined,
broken-down laws which that colony, in common
with its neighbours in Australia, had inherited
from the beloved mother-land, with a view to
repealing the more ancient and useless of the
debris and placing matters on a coherent basis for
the future. The scheme came to nothing, for the
legislators of the island colony quarrelled and
wrangled oyer the deposit of decomposed statutes
which their researches brought to light, and
clawed eloquently at each other about which old
monument should be preserved and which should
be thrown into the ash-heap, and so the shifting
waste of law was left in its ancient, chaotic state,
and nobody knows to this day whether he is
liable to be hanged under an old statute concern-
ing heresy, or is merely exposed to the risk of 25
years’ imprisonment and to lose his ears at the end
of that period, and to be branded in several
places, and then be expelled from the country and
have ail his goods confiscated. The one solid fact
revealed after long research was that there are
enough forgotten laws in existence to hang nearly
everybody, and a few over which would afford
good and sufficient reason to put the survivors
in a large bag along with a grindstone and
drop them at midnight into a swiftly-flowing
river. There are provisions still in force concern-
ing pillories and stocks and thumbscrews, and
elaborate enactments against heretics, and persons
who dabble in occult arts, and individuals who
secretly think evil of the reigning monarch, and
among the du t of the worn-out codes of past ages
there was found one valuable and enlightened
statute setting forth that anyone perpetrating or
attempting to perpetrate an outrage on a female
sovereign shall be burnt alive, after all necessary
steps have been taken, by dislocating the offender
or by boiling oil or other persuasive influences, to
extract information as to the objects which
he had in view. Probably if the investiga-
tion had been carried further, it might have
revealed a few more regulations for remov-
ing the eyes of certain offenders or for extir-
pating the entire families of particularly ob-
noxious criminals, but it stopped short at the law
for burning the person who assaulted a female
sovereign, and the work was never completed.
The scheme for sorting out the tangled labyrinths
of law broke down, apparently because the legis-
lators of New Zealand considered hanging too
light a punishment for such a crime, and didn’t
care to take the responsibility of stating on their
own authority that burning alive was the right
thing to meet the emergency. Probably it never
occurred to them that some compromise might be
effected—say by putting the criminal under the
pump for several hours prior to his execution, or
by making him drink two quarts of water all at
once, or by executing his grandmother along with
him ; nor does it seem to have struck them that
the chances of anything ever happening to a
female sovereign in New Zealand were so exceed-
ingly small that the whole collection of ancient bar-
barisms might as well be wiped off the statute-book
for the honour of the colony, and the sovereign be
allowed to take her chance. As inheritors of the
legal rubbish-heap of Britain, these colonies have
come into possession of a code which is calculated
to make even a cannibal sit up in dismay, and the
only reason why existence is possible under this
grand old system is that three-fourths of the
existing laws are never enforced, and at least half
of them are hardly known to be in existence at
all. But still they do exist, and are ready to be
raked up when a favourable opportunity presents
itself; and meanwhile blatant politicians continue
to rise—after dinner—and, having first got a
secure hold of the table and taken other necessary
steps to prevent the room going round, to pour
forth their admiration of that far-reaching British
constitution which passes all human understand-
ing, and which hangs round in an unobtrusive
fashion with a stake and a bonfire looking for the
man who wants to insult a female sovereign.

Saint McLood.
The saintly Doctor John McLeod, parson and
medico, got married in Sydney some years
ago to a lady who, after listening to his oily
voice, was soft enough to give up £llOO a year
out of £IOOO annual income conditionally
bequeathed to her by her deceased husband,
in order that Dr. McLeod might be her very
own. After a short and tempestuous time they
went to America, where the holy man got
“divorced” without his wife’s knowledge, and
under other apparently fraudulent circumstances.
Yet, however managed, the deed was done in due
legal form, as the copy of the American official
record and the evidence of the IJ.S. Consul at
Sydney seem to show. Thereafter good Doctor
McL eod married in America another lady,
named Cameron —and here comes in the charge
of bigamy on which he was convicted last
week. Now, McLeod couldn't have com-
mitted bigamy if his American divorce was suffi-
cient—and Consul Griffin, who is an American
lawyer, has testified that to the best of his belief
it was valid : anyhow, there is the official divorce
duly signed, sealed and delivered, and as we have
no direct evidence impugning its authenticity, the
prisoner is entitled to the benefit of the doubt. The
Yew South Wales Supreme Court in rejecting the
United States divorce and punishing McLeod
mr bigamy committed in a foreign land has, we
take it, sat in judgment on the decision of a foreign
tribunal. It is all very well to say that, consider-
ing the prisoner’s and witnesses’ statements as to
times and places of domicile, etcetera, the alleged
divorce could not have been legally obtained, but
that is altogether beside the question. How can
you go behind the American Court’s decision
which, it is to be presumed, enabled McLeod
to legally re marry in America ? Nor can you
infer bigamy from the fact that the prisoner
on returning to Sydney, proceeded to blackmail
his first (and as he now says) divorced wife—from
whom he had concealed his alleged divorce-—and
endeavoured to obtain her consent to a res-
cission of the judicial separation which had
been pronounced between them in New South
'vales at her instance. That does not prove
that ho had not been divorced, though it
certainly tends to prove that he was an
unblushing rascal and may help to explain the
enthusiasm with which lie was found guilty by the
jury. His second marriage, like his alleged
divorce, took place in America, and if it was good
enough for the Americans, it is not a trouble
which should be taken on to the shoulders of the
New South Wales taxpayers. How can the New
South Wales Court accept a formal American
marriage as evidence as a man’s guilt, and yet
reject an apparently formal American divorce as
evidence of his innocence? We are sorry for the
Mistresses McLeod, and can only hope that their
sad case will help in the direction of the assimila-
tion of the world’s marriage-laws.

Vale, Henry George.
Henry George’s latest and last Sydney lecture,
entitled “Protection a Fallacy,” was delivered to
a crowded and enthusiastic house. Mr. George,
who has since dined with Mayor Buruekin, a rent-
grabber and land-monopolist, was accompanied to
the platform, not only by his local disciples, but
by several prominent alleged Freetraders, chief
among whom was Mr. G. H. Reid, his chair-
man for the night. The lecture was the
single-tax apostle’s most practical and care-
fully-thought-out Sydney utterance, though
one cannot but smile at the calm manner
in which he, professing to deal with
every question from the standpoint of natural
laws and abstract ideas of justice and freedom,
strives to escape from the cul-de-sac in which that
places him over the Chinese exclusion question, by
discussing it as an expediency the result of the
present unequal and artificial social conditions.
The most ardent Protectionist does no more; he
fully recognises the beauty, the naturalness of
entire freedom of trade in theory, and deplores the
necessity in some cases for prohibitory measures in
practice. As for George’s peroration withits “rally-
ing cry— Freetrade, free land, free men,” it was all
tommyrot. At the beginning of his career the
Prophet of San Francisco started out to annihilate
poverty by the throwing open of natural resources
to all; he has halted half-way, nay turned aside,
and his system is now a mere engine for the raising
of the funds necessary to the carrying on of Govern-
ments ; in fact, he now strives merely to alter a
fiscal system and not the entire social condition.
If you admit the truth of George’s premises that
the land is the property of all and not of indi-
viduals and that its alienation into private hands
is the chief cause of the world’s poverty, then there
can be no logical stopping-place short of the con-
clusions arrived at by his once ardent follower H. O.
Pentecost, who argues that the only valid titles
to land are productive use and residential occupancy
and asserts that all vacant land not reserved for com-
munal use should be at once thrown open to the
selection of the first man who will properly utilise
it. Henry George has told us that the single-tax
will amply provide for all the necessities of the
State, also that its imposition will at once inde-
finitely lower the value of land by abolishing its
selling-value, furthermore that he would only con-
fiscate the unearned increment of the future. We
have in vain challenged Mr. George to produce
his plans and specifications, and we again ask him
to dovetail these three apparently contradictory
propositions.

Two newly-arrived English ladies named Reed,
good and well-intentioned, no doubt, but morally
swivel-eyed all the same, have started lec-
turing in Australia for funds and volun-
teers for some Chinese mission or other.
Let them, instead of tinkering with the
people of another civilization, go back to their
native England, in which country an unhappy,
heroic workman starved himself to death seven
weeks ago that his children might be fed. While
there remains in the fatherland of these ladies one
Caucasian. toiler in such a case, they can well
afford to postpone the task of providing moral
pocket-handkerchiefs for the Chows, who are, to
tell the truth, a far more pious people
than the English, in that J ohn Chinaman
is fool enough to really and truly be-
lieve in Joss and Confucius, and so on;
whereas the hypocritical John Burl obviously
only gammons to believe in the things and per-
sonages he sets up for the edification of the
unsophisticated and imitative people whom he
thrashed into accepting his accursed opium
not many years ago. To verbosely para-
phrase a Bismarckism, the temporal and
eternal welfare of the whole Chinese nation,
rump and stump—past, present, and future—
is not, while the British rich continue to
bury themselves in diamonds and roses and the
poor to rot to death in foetid dens, worth one
bone from the frail skeleton of an English pauper’s
child. Perhaps, however, it will be good to give
a little encouragement to Chinese missions. When
J. Bull gets a fair grip of Chowland, Ah Sin
will begin to guzzle nun and relieve the jim-jams
with morphine; also to scoot with his neighbour’s
wife and start Cleveland-street clubs, after the
fashion of the very best people in England. This
will make J ohnny Atkins Ah Foo very rocky on
his pins and be a distinct handicap on him in the
inevitable march West of the Great Yellow Army
of the near or distant future.

The other day the Sydney police hauled up a man
for trial whoso name they calmly gave from the
official record as “ Smith, alias Tommy the
Burglar.” The Sydney police ought to be kicked
and so ought any judge or justice who permits this
kind of thing. Ltd a prisoner be tried and convicted
first under his own name or the name he gives,
and then let them read out the whole string of
his possibly-police-invcnted aliases if they like. So
far as a square trial goes, you might as fairly
arrest Henry George for sedition and place him
at the bar as “George, alias the Incendiary of
Confiscation,” or dump down a distinguished poli-
tician behind the spikes as “Frogson, alias the
Chronic and Unprincipled Insolvent,” as ticket
an accuse with a damning name. When
next Constable 4298 X is falsely charged with
allowing a prisoner to escape, or with deliberately
knocking a drunken man’s skull against the kerb-
stone, or jumping on him in the cells, let us have
him labelled in the dock as “P.-C. Smith, 4298 X,
alias the Great Pie-Biting Man-Walloper.” It’s
a poor system that won’t work both ways.

New South Wales Attorney-General Simpson has
given way in re his cold-blooded lawyer-like pro-
posal that in erimuial cases women should be corn-
pelled to give evidence against their husbands and
vice-versa. The proposal was alike cruel, stupid
and impracticable. By the time men and women
are schooled up to‘the judicial impartiality of that
Mayor of Galway who hanged his own son, Love,
which helps Hate to rule all things, will have gone
from the world ; and there is no one who ought to
know better than the courtly G. B. Simpson that
love, like hate, is eternal. Any man who is human
will commit a million prejuries to save the wife
he loves; any woman, who as a woman is
worth a curse, will perjure herself on ten thousand
cartloads of Bibles to save her children and her
husband —even if she does not really love
him, but only loves her amour proprc. You
have only to look at the records of the Divorce-
courts, or if you like the records of the Courts of
Petty Sessions in their bastardy jurisdiction, to
see what hopeless perjury is inevitably the result
of oases directly involving sexual relations. How
can you establish or punish perjury-in such cases ?
Look at the great Gympie case the other day. The
respondent and co-respondent solemnly asserted
that they had not committed adultery, yet the
jury found to the contrary and fined Tronson
£2250. Who thinks of prosecuting Tuonson for
the perjury he must have committed if the jury,
whose verdict is final, found a right verdict ? in
this regard let the unsentimental law continue in
its world-old track—a track hard-beaten by the
footsteps of a hundred generations of jurists ere
there was born into the world anybody by the
name of Simpson.

Michafl B 4RRY has been hanged at Foe' hampton
(Q.) after being found guilty on the direction of a
judge who took it upon himself to say straight out
ere the man had been convicted, that it was the
jury’s bounden duty to cast him for death. The
execution carries with it no moral for the public
good, except the old one on which The Bulletin
has so often insisted—that as juries’recommenda-
tions to mercy are almost uniformly rejected, the
people must take out of the hands of the judge
and put into the hands of the jury the power of
fixing sentences. Barry’s deed, brutal as it was,
was clearly the act of an irresponsible man, and
his end will deter no other man of his mental
calibre from doing just what he is goaded on to do
by drink or any other sufficient exciting cause. The
gallows never deterred a murderer since the year
One, B.c. The man who really means to slay
either is bent on revenge and does not care—until
after the deed, when his fear comes too late ; or
he reckons on escaping detection —every criminal
thinks himself so much cleverer than every other
preceding one, and has before him examples which
he thinks will enable him to avoid the pitfalls into
which his predecessors slipped. As conscience
make cowards, so custom makes brutes. The
spectacle of the State hiring a man-butcher to
choke a citizen and then hump away his carcase
from the gallows, like the corpse of a slaughtered
sheep, is, so far from its being a deterrent, a mere
subsidy to the Spirit of Assassination. Civiliza-
tion is not even skin-deep, and the crowd, being
debarred from enjoying the actual spectacle-
edifying in theory—of a fellow-creature being done
to death, patiently loaf outside the judicial
abattoirs at the time of an execution, or gloat over
the newspaper reports afterwards.

T ifteen drapers of >St. Kilda (Mclb. ) have vindi-
cated Deakin's Early Closing Act in a manner
that should effectually shut up its Tory opponents.
The local council of St. Kilda borough took upon
themselvf s, as many other councils have done, to
nullify the Act of Parliament with a miserable
little by-law, extending the compulsory closing
time by two hours on five nights of the week and
one hour on Saturdays. That is to say, the
council fixed the times at 9 o’clock and 11 o’clock,
instead of 7 and 10. The fifteen drapers aforesaid
now pray for a repeal of the by-law, so that they,
and their competitors, may be amenable to the
statute. They have tried late closing and find
that “it is not worth their while keeping open
after the statutory hours.” It is not pretended
that the drapers are suffering from high motives,
or drew up their petition in a spirit of special
kindness to their employes. They merely testify
to the fact that their customers have adopted a
custom of early shopping which makes the old late
closing game no longer worth the candle —or the
gas.
The Victorian Ministry is likely to be (or may
already have been) cornered by the want of confi-
dence motion which “Charley” Taylor substi-
tuted for Munro’s original flabby indictment.
“That the expenditure of the colony has been
unduly increased owing to the misleading state-
ment of the Treasurer in the last session of Parlia-
ment” is a truth so pure and beautiful that only
the cast-iron conscience of political partisanship
could withstand it. In short, if Parliament were
what it professes to be, viz., a body of patriots
voting according to their honest convictions, the
amendment would be seconded by Duncan
Gillies himself, and carried unanimously. It is
seldom that an amendment embodies a plain,
straightforward fact, admitting of no ingenious
contortions or legitimate discussion. The pot
calls the kettle black, and the Ministerial utensil
is black. Yet, as members in all parts of the
House are relying upon the Coalition Government
to stifle the Premier Permanent Society prosecu-
tions, they will perhaps see cause for maintaining
the kettle’s purity a while longer.
Men who know something about the subject will
tell you that the “ Sweating System ” in the Mel-
bourne tailoring trade (and a very limited system
it is) has its origin in the chuckle-headed obstinacy
of the old-fashioned “home ” tailor. None of the
big tailoring firms give any facilities for working
in other but the cross-legged style, so that Polish
Jews and all varieties of (icings who have followed
their craft in the States, are forced to work at
home, where they can stand at a table and get
through 50 per cent, more sewing than the miser-
able worms who squat upon a floor because it was
the custom of their ancestors. Working tailors
appear to be divided into two classes in Melbourne
or any other Australian town—dull, slow English-
men, and intelligent, greasy foreigners. Among
these latter we don’t include a few smart Ameri-
cans. Of young colonials there are few who
wear a tailor’s thimble, either sitting or standing.
They may aspire to cut clothes but they show no
native ambition to make ’em up, which, consider-
ing that a “ first-class ” suit is occasionally hitched
together for 10s. fid., shows that the rising genera-
tion knows its way along the paths of industry
and doesn’t get bogged on a bad road.

Lecturing on Australian Nationality at Charters
Towers, Canon Tucker, among other sensible
things, remarked that—
The only difference between man and man that thev
could acknowledge was one of function. In the way of
illustration take the (Jueeu of England and the poor
washerwoman who toils from morning till night in an
endeavour to gain the means of sustenance. Which was
the better woman? What was the difference between
the two? In what did they differ? Function.
The better woman would be she who did her
work best. (Applause.) There were Australians
who dreamt of an Australian monarchy, with ill
ordered grades of nobles, with its titles, its privileges
its ribbons, its crosses, and its stars. They who follow
the star which Milton’s soul for Shkllky’s lighted, ha\ a
another, and, as they thought, a nobler vision, lie did
not believe in conferring on any man distinction above
another. He did not believe in a one-man Government,
a one-man church, a one-man institution of any kind.
Titles were odious and absurd. He did not like to hear
the terms “ honourable,” “ lord,” “ reverend,” he did not
agree with, he could not see the use of giving to a man
titles he in no way earned. They saw' an Australia, in
which was neither privilege or rank, in which no man-
made distinctions separated man from man ; in which
there were no nobles save those of nature’s making ; but
in which all women were peers, pares, equal; in which
every office was open to every man and woman ; in which
goodness gave the greatness, and the only honour ren-
dered was that which was due to merit. (Tremendous
applause.) Was not such a state worth working, waiting,
praying for? (Applause.) Better men than they had
thought it worth dying for. (Cheers.) Republicans know
of no authority in matters political, save that of the per-
sons whom the matters concern.”
The Bulletin has not lived in vain ; the fiery
cross of Republican propaganda is passing from
hand to hr-ul. Canon Tucker’s reference to the
bebaubled dreams of the Australian Tory party,
reads strangely like a satire on the picture drawn
by Parkes —in one of the first of his Federation
speeches—of the painted glories of the Royal
Governor-General’s “Court.”
Mr. E. Hulme, of King River, Oxley (Vic.),
writes to the Australasian -
I know from 25 years’ Victorian experience that where-
ever the vine has been cultivated sufficiently to produce
wine, drunkenness has fearfully increased, and in the
district from which 1 write, every wine-maker has been
“ bitten by his own dog,” and in taking a hair of the
same dog, it has proved rabid, and they have all died
miserable drunkards, prematurely, with but one excep-
tion, and he sold out, but is still a confirmed drunkard ;
and this testimony is borne out to a very great extent in
our larger wine-growing districts, which anyone can see
for themselves who will take the trouble. It is all
“bosh” to say a people will keep sober by drinking
natural wine “ at all times ” of from 25deg. to 30deg. of
alcoholic strength.
Then Mr. Hulme artlessly goes on to try and
prove by “authorities ” that a similar state of
things exists in the wine-growing countries of
Europe. The truth of the matter is simply this ;
The millions of the wine-consumers of, say, France,
drink habitually from their childhood light wines
which, before dilution, contain from 9 to 13 per-
cent. alcohol—and do themselves far less harm than
is done by tea-drinking elsewhere. The average
local drinker of Australian vintages takes heavy
syrupy wines of over 25 per cent, alcoholic strength
—he has unfortunately not yet got over the
poisonous port and sherry and madeira habit
which he inherited from J. Bull, along with the
heavy frock-coat and the cast-iron plum-pudding
and the ghastly black hat. The Bulletin pre-
sumes that Mr. Hulme’s letter is written in the
teetotal interest. So far as the dangers of drinking
sound H;/ht wines arc concerned, Mr. Hulme
might, following the lead of Bishop Barry, have
far better attacked the vice of over-feeding, brink
slays its scores, gluttony its hundreds.
An idea of the sort of law which obtains
in West Australia is given by a recent Perth
police-court case. James Bloomer was charged by
Andrew Dyson with leaving his employ without
giving a week’s notice. The complainant said he
engaged the defendant as a ’bus driver, and he was
a weekly servant, and had left him without giving
proper notice. The magistrate s.vd that defend-
ant was liable to a fine of £5O, or three months'
imprisonment, but as complainant did not wish to
press the charge ’ he would be fined £l, or one
month’s imprisonment.
A country paper points out that Australian
civil servants could best show their loyalty to the
Grown not by taking two holidays for the Birthday
of Queen Victoria, but by “putting in ” a few
hours extra service occasionally, free of charge.
Just so, but then again a Q’sland magistrate has
discharged a lot of drunks whose excuse was that
they had been drinking Her Rubicund Oleaginous-
ness’s health. Twas ever thus. Monarchy from
its inception has been a vehicle for debauchery,
crime and every weakness or villainy of which man
is capable. When the Republic comes drunken-
ness on a Royal birthday will be deemed an aggra-
vation of the offence, unless the inebriate can show
that he was drinking to the deep damnation of all
crowned heads and sich.
The brave, unselfish, old Gustave Techow,
gymnast and gentleman, is dead. He was ex-
cluded from the amnesty granted to political
prisoners on the accession of the lately defunct
German Emperor Frederick, for reasons set forth
in The Bulletin at the time, and, consequently,
the dearest wish of his heart, that he should,
after an expatriation of over 40 years, breathe his
last in the Fatherland for which he fought and
bled was unrealised. Techow, who united in his
person all the most noble attributes of German
manhood, was an object-lesson in patriotism—
patriotism of the Kossuth stamp —and down in
the old galvanised-iron rockery by the East Mel-
bourne cricket-ground he taught a lot more than
the “ bars ”or the trapeze. Many a young Vic-
torian relaxed his grip on “the Throne” as a
corollary to his attendance on the tuition of
Gustave Techow.
There died last week in Hobart a man
with a record. 'lhomas James Crouch, formerly
Under-Sheriff of Tasmania, had attended more
executions than any other man who ever lived
south of the equator. He had read their death-
warrants to over 400 condemned men. It was he
who conferred his appointment on Solomon Blay,
the white-headed Tasmanian hangman, who has
slung about 210 souls into the next world. Outside
of his office, old Crouch was the brightest and
most genial of men, and was a genuine philanthro-
pist on a small scale. The sudden death of his
son, the well-known Melbourne architect, a short
tune ago hastened Ids own end. But for that sad
event it is possible he, being a pensioner, might
have realised his own jocular prophecy—that he
would see the century out. He threw a shoe after
Sir Alfred Stephen on the occasion of the latter’s
marriage with Parson Bedford’s daughter.
A correspondent addresses The Bulletin
on an interesting historical matter :
Editor Bulletin, —Sir,—Will you allow me to correct
a small error which appeared in the kast issue of your
paper ? Referring to an old man named George Swanson,
you state that “ Swanston’’-street, Melbourne, was
named after him, the“t” having crept in by mistake.
The name, however, is spelt quite correctly, for it was
named, not after Swanson, hut after my grandfather Cap-
tain Swanston, who, together with Captain Montague,
Messrs. Gellibrand and Mercer, formed a company in Tas-
mania for the purpose of taking up land at Port Phillip,
as it was then called, and sent over Batman to deal with
the natives, they being unaware at the time that the British
Government claimed sovereignty over the whole of
Australia.
The George Swanson referred to plied a ferry, I believe,
where Prince’s Bridge now stands, and afterwards kepi
a draper’s shop in Swanston-street. There is another
Swanston-street in Geelong, and in one or two up-countrv
towns Mounts Gellibrand and Mercer were named after
other members of the company.— l am, &c.. Giias. Swans-
ion, J.P., L.R.C.P. and S., Kd.

beyond his signature ! Education is at a discount
after all. At a loan and discount, in fact.

Traill in buttoned-up democratic-looking ulster of a viru-

in a d.c. box and went out regularly at every interval—
to see how the new tram-lettering worked, I suppose.
The house was crowded, and the girl with her hair in
sausage-roll puffs and an old gown on had the pull of the
syren who had gotten herself up in her best confection
but neglected her cranium, since one notices coiffures
more than costumes when things are S. R. O. There
seemed to be rivers of red—one charmer in true Jubilee
with a cloak lined with rabbit-skins irresistibly reminded
me of a flayed sheep with part of his coat left hanging,
and another in the giddy Scriptural hue obscured the
stage with the Eiffelesque tangle of her skulling. And a
nice young man of the Passover-cake variety had his
cape-overcoat lined with scarlet—to resemble the cloak of
the naughty Baron Scarpia, I suppose, and I felt quite
sorry that the resemblance ceased at the lining—but, then,
Bellew has probably rehearsed the graceful hang of his top
wrap.
The main drawbacks to the “ Mdile. de Bressier”
premier at the Royal on 28th ult were the dozens of sad
black gowns relieved with lace and jet with aggravating
regularity. Then Rupert Carington was there like a rose
between two buds—one bud wearing flesh-pink, and the
other black, with a mauve corsage bouquet. Seeing how
exquisitely “ Mdile ” is staged—the boudoir and studio
scenes being especially convincing—it was soothing to
see that Mrs. 8.-P.’s wardrobe was in complete harmony
with the setting, though really all the toilettes—being
decidedly of to-day—were arch»ologically incorrect, as
the action takes place during the Commune—twenty
years ago. In the second act, when she first appears, the
gown proper is in soft creamy stuff, with Y-shaped
neck-piece of fawn guipure, the skirt edged with guipure
at the hem; belt and sash of heliotrope-pink. Over this
is worn a kind of cloak in fawn woollen with embroidered
border, the Zouave fronts lined with white and tied with
white ribbon ; the long turned-back wing sleeves with a
deep white border. At the bottom the cloak is divided
and lined with white satin ; at the neck it is gathered at
the back into a high semi-collar lined with white, which
produces a charming frontal effect. A large floral hat,
one mass of crimson roses and leaves and bows of white
ribbon is worn with this. When Mrs. Potter came forth
to bow her acknowledgements and collect the vegetables
(a large wreath of choice blooms being especially pro-
minent) she left the cloak off and appeared in the cream
gown only. In the studio scene the dressing is sublimely
artistic, and Mrs. Potter looks her very prettiest and acts
her very best besides. The gown is simple, of course—if
you notice, most truly artistic gowns cost about sixpence
a yard—and is in soft brown woollen en Princesse
with sides of seal - brown velvet. The bodice is
in semi-circular folds of the woollen with V of brow
velvet at the back and V of brown and gold beading at
the front and belt of black satin ; picture sleeves of seal-
brown velvet with woollen cuffs. Over this, on entering,
is worn a black cloak with a bold design in passementerie
and a fawn felt hat of the saggy variety adorned with
brown velvet and shaded brown ostrich feathers. To give
a needed touch of colour a terra-cotta scarf is held in a
purposeless way where it will do most good. In act four,
the gowning is mourning, the only touch of colour being
the bright hair of the worried demoiselle, which was worn
<1 la bird’s-nest-after-a-free-fight, as usual. In the last
act, Miss Gwynne (why ever does she put two daubs of
pipeclay on her eyelids and a lick of stove-polish under
her nose ?) wears a pretty gown in heliotrope with per-
pendicular bars of bronze velvet ; but Jenny Watt-Tanner
(who got a reception) in a pinky-creamy horror looked
(on this occasion) like that festive “ widow in reduced
circumstances who takes one gentleman boarder.”
Summarising the glories of the gowns Mrs. Brown-
Potter has favoured us with lately, your Sappho has come
to the conclusion that their success is due to the fact that
you are never sure when the wearer will take something
off or put something on, or juggle with the sash-end and
so change the whole apparent idea that you never knew
when you have really grasped the full meaning of the
toilet. And this appeals, for when the drapery is artistic,
“ versatility of motive,” as one may say, is decidedly
fetching, and I sincerely hope that local society, which
never gets beyond two colours combined in the most
ordinary and foreseen way will take a lesson and emanci-
pate itself at last from the alphabet of full dress where it
is floundering at present. I also noticed that the B.P.
wardrobe lent itself readily to resurrection—one gown
coming out in as many disguises (which didn’t save it from
the eagle optic of your Sappho) as a fashionable absconder
en route to Honolulu.
The Southern Amateur Theatrical Society gave another
evidence of its being strong and well at Sydney School of
Arts on 29th ult., when *’ Milky White ” and the “ Rose of
Auvergne” were very well rendered indeed, in the
presence of a crammed house headed by Mayor and Mrs.
Burdekin. The most life like character on the boards was
the Dick Dugs of A. Ellice-Flint, who carried on
generally as the degraded object he was supposed to be ;
but the “ star” wasn’t letter-perfect, and the fascinating
jeune premier in a straw hat and a lisp hovered anxiously
at the wings with his ear and eye on the busy prompter.
The proceedings were of the most rekerky description
“ in front,” nearly' everyone wearing war-paint.
And, alluding to manners, how do you like the two
latest hand-shakes from England ? To engineer one you
bold your band on a level with your adversary’s nose with
ibe fingers drooping. If the adversary is in the know.
•he elevates her fist to a similar altitude and lightly
squeezes the greeting finger-tips ; but if she isn’t—both
parties retire with wrecked paw-cases, that’s aIL Grip
No. 2 is more “inward” and appeals to the subterranean
emotions of unappreciated matrons and responsive
adorers, by whom it is much affected. This is the how of
it: You extend the arm straight in front, seize as much
of your opponent’s claw as you possibly can with a
crescetido con-expressionc squeeze and fine, gentle, mean-
ing pumps, and there you are. Time, one and a-ha.f
seconds, but may be extended during the absence of the
old man.
There is an estimable institution called the “ Ladies’
Dressmaking Association ” in the Victoria Arcade, Sydney.
It was started some years ago by a committee of philan-
thropic women, and is patronised, I am told, by people
who are interested in the welfare of girls who are not well-
learned enough for the miserable teaching profession, but
for whom, all the same, the ordinary workroom is an
impossible outlet. This praiseworthy movement stands in
need of a little patronage just now. Its main object is to
give employment to the awkwardly-situated “shabby-
genteel,” who would otherwise turn “ companion,” or
lady-help, or something equally overdone—and probably
see their elbows working through their sleeves as they
crowded round the registry-offices, waiting for employ
ment that might never come. In itself dressmaking is a
rather nice business, and as the pay after the months dur-
ing which the novice is getting her hand in is £1 per week
the hours from nine to five, and no girl is admitted unless
Well-recommended by the committee, the elected may
consider themselves fortunate. It’s a worthy struggle,
isn’t it ? and it’s a pity more people don’t give them their
orders, since the more work in hand the more hands in
work there—though some hands may thus lose work else-
where. Lady Bertie’s bridesmaids’ dresses were made by
the Association, and they are now finishing a very elegant
gown in white moire-pearl passementerie and sap-green
velvet for a bride-elect. Everybody really interested in
the “ What can we do with our girls?” conundrum should
give the Association a trial, as their charge is only thirty-
nine and sixpence for the make, linings, and etceteras,
and the cutter is a London professional, who regards a
badly-hung skirt as a cardinal sin.
Have you remarked what “good form” vulgarity o*
demeanour is just now? Not so very long ago for a
woman to cross her—well—knee or turn her head in public
was an unpardonable solecism, but now we have arms
akimbo, arms folded, knees folded and heads rotating
until anybody out of the Movement would want to turn
round three times and think it out in the refrigerator. The
Countess of Hopetoun generally wears one hand on her
hip—all the vice-regalities slip one knee over the other—
and with these unimpeachable precedents society has
thrown the old poker-backed doctrines into the fire and
carries its limbs anyhow, that is to say, if they are any
way presentable. The reason for this laxity of deport-
ment is, that when once a certain section of the “lowah
ordahs ” comprehends that a lady should resemble a petri-
fied image which clasps its hands below its diaphragm and
doesn’t move a hair even if a bundle of crackers went off
under its perch, they set about doing likewise; and as
nothing is easier to behave as a perfect lady on “Manual’’
lines, the result is that the duchesses of the draperies
come forward with a graceful glide and supply you with a
packet of pins in the most approved boudoir manner. Of
course society cannot share its little ways with “people”
who should always turn their toes in, and are under a
moral obligation to eat peas with their knife from father
to son, but if they will improve and fold their hands
gracefully in public, why there is nothing for it but what
has already happened-a sudden adaptation of posed
arms and ankles on the side of the defence. There !
isn’t that a beautifully logical exegesis of the raison
d’etre of the weird gymnastics of local culchaw ?
Partly owing to the draggle-tailed sort of day and partly
owing to the Randwick races, which latter event lent
itself better to the gambling mania, the Championship
Athletic Meeting at Sydney Association Ground wasn’t
attended by a particularly well-dressed aggregate and no
very special frocks were flaunted. Neither Lady Caring,
ton nor Mrs. Brown-Potter, both of whom had accepted
the club’s invitation, were discernible with the naked eye
anywhere about the premises, though Baron Carington, in
a topper and a jubilee necktie, skirmished around in a
general way for a while and made the proceedings V.R.
Mayoress Burdekin wore navy-blue cloth with pinked-out
tan cloth edgings, small cream-lace-and-ribbon bonnet,
and cream lace parasol. Mrs. Carruthers, dark-green
cloth with borderings of beaver fur; picture hat in felt
with green ostrich feathers. A costume of black
velvet, made on the porter-bottle plan, with a
rust-brown felt hat and feather and a brown moire
gamp was nice in a scenic sense ; but a lady in moonshine-
green cut Y-shape and a flower in her pokeish hat was
enough to give a conventional person the jumps. On the
track the dressing was occasionally d6gag6, and I shall
never forget the look of a long knock-kneed Maori with a
yard of bare whip-handle shanks and a split top-coat jog-
ging to scratch in the teeth of the wind. And I didn’t
like the New Zealanders’ uniforms—black with a sort of
skull and bone on the left side—it looked like an animated
coffin with the name-plate askew ; but the Queenslanders’
snowy raiment was like faint lilies, and looked simply
soulful—where it wasn’t soiled. By the bye, when will
the Association have the garden in the ladies’ reserve
attended to ? At present it’s a tangle of ugly weeds which
have killed off all the poor little plantlets and riot even
on to the gravel.

Merchant Tronson and his mature ‘ ‘ mash, ”
to whose injured husband he has now to pay £2250
divorce-damages, had for their chief rendezvous a
grave in the old Gympie cemetery where the giddy
man had five, and his charmer two children
buried. Tronson’s version of the matter was that
he used to sit there in the gloaming and relate to
her harmless little anecdotelets about his trip to
England. He ought to have said that they talked
of nothing but the weather, and the jury might
have believed him. Some juries will believe any-
thing.
Neat par from a down South (Vic.)
paper:—
John Coherty has instructed a highly respectable
firm of solicitors (as solicitors go), to demand our
apology for mentioning in our last issue that the proper
pronunciation of his (Ooherty’s) name sounds uncommonly
like “dirty.” The respectable firm of solicitors further
desire us to give them one guinea, at which price we
should regard their letter as an extravagantly overpaid
piece of literature. However, if Mr. Coherty is aggrieved
at his name standing like “dirty,” we beg to express our
regret that we wounded his feelings, and also to express
an opinion that his name ought to have been “shirty.”
As regards that guinea subscription to the solicitors, we
are reluctantly compelled to mention that our last twenty-
one shillings have been invested in a bull-pup, a faithful
animal which will do its level best to lay hold upon the
leg of any man who imagines mischief and vain things
against our royal person.
A party of six Australian citizens received
an unlooked-for visit one night not long ago, and.
none of them have yet recovered from the effects
They were sitting peacefully together playing-
euchre by the light of a slush lamp, which smelt
as if something was dead, after the fashion of
slush lamps in general, when a stranger came in
quite casually and neglected to offer any coherent
observation worth mentioning. He got in with
difficulty owing to the scantiness of the available
space, but the proprietors of the premises left at
once and unanimously in order to make room for
him. One of them retired through the window
amid a general smash and scatteration of glass
and everything else, and two went up the chimney
—one standing unconsciously in the fire while the
other crowded up and left the way clear for him
to follow. A fourth made his unassuming exit
through the side of the house itself, and carried
most of it along with him —not that he particularly
wanted the side of anybody’s house, but the
article happened to be standing in the way, and
he carried it off in a moment of abstraction, just as
he might have removed an anchor, or a quantity
of red-hot iron, or an infuriated lion if he had
been thinking of something else at the moment.
As for the other two, they offered up a piercing
shriek apiece and got out anyhow. They have no
absolutely reliable recollections of how they did
it, but one of them fancies that he burrowed
rapidly through the ground with his nails and
haying passed underneath the foundations of the
building he came up in haste at a considerable
distance, and as he is unable to find the hole
through which he made his exit, he is inclined to
believe he must have stopped to fill it up again
without - being aware of the fact ; and the other
suffers from an impression that he travelled
through the air, and neglected to come down
again for 150 yards. All of the party are more or
less frayed and torn through concussing against
everything that came in their way, and the entire
six are much out of breath and have remained in
that state for 42 days. Their home was a one-
roomed wooden shanty on the bank of a river, and it
was furnished with a table, a window and chimney,
and a door that opened towards the slimy stream
on the very edge of the bank. It was small—
so small that a man could break the window
with one hand and drag the door off its hinges
with the other, and it was inconveniently crowded.
Four of the company were playing euchre, and
the other two were merely swearing and using
obscene language in a general sort of way. When
things were at this stage the bald man in the
brown suit who sat nearest the door turned up a
club, and observed that he would take it up and
that any miserable lost soul in the company who
tried to play against his hand would have reason
to wish that his parents had never become
acquainted with each other, and just then a strange,
muffled voice behind him said something in an un-
dertone which seemed to imply that it endorsed
these sentiments and would also feel inclined to
take it up under similar conditions. Presently,
hovyever, the bald man lost, and his partner rose
excitedly to state that he was the very last earthly
possibility as one of the lower animals in disguise,
and pointed out that in his capacity as an ass
he put a finish and completeness on the
business which had never been equalled ; and
the voice outside was generally understood to
smile at these observations and to encourage
the speaker to go on. Then the bold victim of in-
sult and outrage said that if the person outside
was prepared to maintain the attitude ho was sup-
posed to have taken up in the course of his recent
remarks he had better come in and do it, and the
unseen one was generally understood to reply that
he was coming in as fast as he could manage, and
that he would come faster if he only knew how.
Just here the little, round, fat, oily man next the
fireplace said that he would also take it up, and
the invisible speaker offered a sound which seemed
to say that he had made it a rule to take it up all
his life, and that he was coming in presently to
take it up more than ever. The little, oily man,
however, was also euchred, and gave way to
language which was fearful to listen to, and
the intrusive person in the background in-
serted a muffled yelp which occupied an
unauthorised position in the programme of
the small man’s profanity, and seemed to
imply that he regarded that person with loathing
and contempt. Then a long, silent citizen, who
so far had played his cards without a word of
comment, rose and said that he considered the
time had arrived to put a head on the man outside,
and also stated that he would reduce that person
to such a state that his own mother would pass
him by without recognition, and for reply there
came in a long wheeze and sigh which appeared to
set forth that the visitor was an orphan and didn’t
care a cuss, anyhow. Just here the man who sat
next the door rose four feet in the air all at once
and submitted a fearful, splitting, unearthly
shriek for the consideration of the company, who
seemed to regard it as rather a rare specimen and
to wonder where he got it from. Then, briefly re-
marking that there was a large alligator coming in
at the door, he fled np the chimney, and his com-
panions started madly after him in the various
ways aforementioned, and proceeded to swallow
time and space in the wild velocity of their retreat.
And as they left there rose behind them a sound
like a horse’s cough, which seemed to embody a
statement to the effect that the alligator had just
dropped in while passing and was sorry they
couldn’t stay.

The performance of “ Mdlle. de Bressier,”
at Sydney Royal, was chiefly remarkable for its
re-introduction, after a long absence, of Jenny
Watt-Tanner, who was very warmly received, to
the Sydney stage ; the estimation in which it was
held by the public may be guaged by its with-
drawal after three nights in favour of “ David
Garrick,” who was succeeded by “ La Tosca on
Thursday evening, and on Friday by “Camille,”
which play rings down the curtain on the Potter-
Bellew season, which has been in every way a most
successful one. We scarcely think Delpit or his
translator Merivale can be responsible for the
clumsiness of the maudlin story depicted at the
Royal. The last act is particularly mixed ; for
example, the widowed heroine, whose father and
brother were killed by the Communists, is about to
be betrothed to a young sculptor who had fought
as a Communist, and whose father was shot dur-
ing the Civil War at the door of his son’s lady-
love, who had betrayed his hiding-place. Folks
were just beginning to think that all the
bother was over and that they might haul on their
wraps, when it was discovered that the wicked
husband’s body (he had mysteriously disappeared
in Algeria), had never been found, therefore that
no inquest had been held, consequently that no
proofs of his death existed, ergo, that no marriage
could take place. This complication disposed of
by the opportune confession of his murderer, an-
other difficulty arose from the fact that the parent
of one party to the contract was a Communist, that
of the other an armed upholder of “ law-’n-
order.” This was settled somehow, and then
the sculptor’s mother chipped in and denounced
the young woman as the murderess of her
husband, and the audience began to think they’d
get home next week, but the business was settled
by the couple standing firm while the old lady
capitulated, and the curtain descended slowly on
the usual “I am thine for ever ” business. Mrs.
Potter in the name-part was fairly successful,
Jenny Watt-Tanner (albeit her make-up in the
first act was too youthful) was a satisfactory Mme.
Rosny, and as Aurelie and Nelly the Misses
Kinnaird and Gwynne filled the bill. As Jacques,
Kyrle Bellew got most of the grease-paint and
limelight, and Edward Sass as Pierre, in accor-
dance with his usual custom, got killed off early.
Toole opens on Saturday in “A Pool and His
Money ” and “Ici on Parle Francais.”
