At Her Majesty’s, the “ Two Orphans”
has very much improved all round since first
night. Maggie Oliver’s Frochard is now a com-
plete impersonation of the heartless old hag who
has but one soft corner in her heart. Jewett’s
democratic speech, as de Yaudray, in the Chateau,
is impressively delivered ; he has a good companion
picture in the Marquis dePresles of Henry Hoyte.
A. E. Greenaway, however, continues' to shed
tears without intermission right through until lie
sniffs battle, when he rises superior to snivel and
proceeds to shed blood. The “ Two Orphans,”
after.a three weeks run, retire in favour of “After
Dark,” on Saturday evening.

At Sydney Criterion “ Fun on the Bristol”
has drawn large audiences throughout a
run of twenty-four nights, and the only com-
plaint is that folks don’t see enough of the
cleverly-impersonated Widdy O’Brien. During
the week the olio business has been completely
changed, and the eccentric dancing of Harry
Crawford, whose high-kicking excels that of the
Girards, is a feature of the show. The com-
pany—their vacation of the theatre is compulsory
—skip to Newcastle on Saturday, where they play
the “Bristol” and good, old “Uncle Tom,” anil
the gap will be filled by that detachment of the
Brough-Boucicault Co. whose names we catalogued
last week in the “ Parvenu, ” a piece in which the
lovers of comedy can count on seeing Anson, who
has returned in rare form, at his best.

Little seem to have accurately guaged the taste of
the public, for “Judge-not” has been drawing

ances in the latter play at the Opera House.

At Bondi Aquarium, in spite of rain,
there was a big muster last Sunday when the
admission-by-invitation system was satisfactorily
introduced. A lengthy concert-programme by a
number of Sydney’s leading vocalists was gone
through, and the offertory was a bumper. On
Saturday W. B. Warner, better known as
“Billy,” takes a benefit, when thirty performers
have volunteered their services. The divine
William, it is said, goes to England to paralyse
them at the Pavilion and the Canterbury.

In the first reception of “ Joseph’s Sweet-
heart ” at Melbourne Bijou, there was every pro-
mise of good times in store for a play which lias
already earned a giddy reputation in Sydney. Its
sweet superfluity of naughtiness and picturesque
appointments will doubtless commend this dis-
jointed yarn to all save the rigidly virtuous, who
may be expected to form themselves into vigilance
committees for the purpose of coming often, in
order to feel assured that “Joseph and his Sweet-
heart ” continually get right through the valley of
temptation without falling by the way. The un-
regenerate majority addicted to guffawing at
Joseph’s inhuman indifference to woman’s wiles,
and at a loss to understand why a noble lord
should waste his blandishments upon a fragile little
figure from the waxworks, such as Fanny appears to
be, willfind no fault with Buchanan’s second adapta-
tion from Fielding, beyond that its interest is arti-
ficial, its heroics altogether shams, and its spiciest
situation a reminder of “ Sophia.” The dramatist,
in fact, anticipated the character of Lady Booby


is not a photograph, nor a photo, a ghost—but a good photo, is a
true reflection of the original. For high-art photos, go to the
London Palace of Art, 10 Park-street, Sydney. Country photographers’ work from developemen
to completion executed at lowest prices. Bromides a speciality, Staff of artists kept for working up.

The late klir (then Mister) John O’Shanassy
estimated, in 1856, that the Victorian population
would be six millions by this time, wherefore he
launched out with J. G. Knight’s magnificent
plans for Parliament Houses.
Mount Morgan (Q.) gold-mine has up to
now paid two million pounds sterling in dividends;
Broken Hill Proprietary silver-mine (N.S.W.) has
in five years just paid four millions of pounds
profit, and the experts say that they have as yet
“ merely scratched the surface.”
It must be conceded that that enthusiastic
teetotal vegetarian, Cyril Haviland, is in his
right element as secretary of Sydney local
School of Arts, which, for the first time in its
history, is being properly boomed. Fifteen-
hundred subscribers have been added to the list
within the year. A new. and effective system for
the receiving and delivery of books has been in-
vented. The subscription for ladies has been re-
duced, and all works of modern and popular
authors have had to be multiplied fourfold.
We have received a handsomely-bound
book on the “ Jonolan Caves ” from the pen of J.
J. Foster, who chaperoned Henry George through
Australia, issued by order of Minister Sydney
Smith from the N.S.W. Government Printing
Office. Mr. Foster spent over six weeks in ex-
ploring the oaves, during which time he estimates
that he crawled through twenty miles of subter-
ranean passages and was the first visitor to
enter some new caves which he has appro-
priately named. Those appellations, as well
as some he conferred on certain longer-known
but unnamed caverns, have been adopted as
the official designations and all the measure-
ments, &c., have been verified by a Government
surveyor. Mr. Foster lias been remarkably suc-
cessful in describing the caves in simple yet
graphic language while avoiding tiresome repeti-
tions : however, had the book been illustrated,
its value as a means of introducing picturesque
Australia to the world’s notice would have been
greatly increased. The author leaves for England,
via America, in search of novelties next week.
You really ought to drink Wolfe’s Schnapps.
No sufferer from any scrofulous disease, who
will fairly try Ayer’s Sarsaparilla, need despair of
a cure. This remedy purges the blood of im-
purities, destroys the germs of scrofula, and in-
fuses new vigor throughout the system.
Wolfe's Schnapps for lowness of spirits.
Use Batho’s Baking Powder.
in Lady Bellaston, and just as he drew upon
“ Joseph Andrews ” in compiling “ Sophia,” so he
throws back to “Tom Jones” for the Lord
Fellamar who meditates the conquest of Joseph’s
weird mash with chicken and champagne at Rane-
lagh Gardens. This scene of dazzling light, by
the way, fell short of expectation. There was a
strange dearth of waiters at the Gardens, and no
incidental signs of gaiety to account for the pre-
sence of a mob of improper females in masks. No-
body appeared to be drinking anything, the
mechanical laughter of the aforesaid syrens palled
upon the ear, and a general suspicion of sepulchral
gloom haunted the entire reckless dissipation. For
the rest, “Joseph’s Sweetheart” is admirably
mounted, besides being prettily grouped and cos
turned. It only requires a proper infusion of
snuff-taking and a lot more bad language to com-
plete the last-century illusion.
# * *
As regards the acting, Mrs. Brough’s im-
provement in the delineation of unrequited love,
since Lady Bellaston was “ froze off ” by Tom
Jones, is most pleasingly apparent. She plays a
difficult part with all the warmth and dash that
good taste will allow, yet the unfortunate effect of
her artistic success in the first act is to make Mr.
Cecil Ward an object of contempt for the rest of
the evening. Everybody feels that he ought to be
made flunkey to Sydney Social Purity Society, and
provided with a gimlet. Fanny is likewise un-
supported by public sympathy in her tribulation,
not because her sentiments are virtuous, but be-
cause she doesn’t strike one as being a real
live girl, any more than Athena Claudius
is a real live name. Robert Inman, again,
is universally derided for his idiocy in
chasing this phantom so persistently, though
otherwise he deserves a word of praise.
Miss Romer seems bound to become a prime
favourite in the part of Mrs. Slipshod, and
the gipsy of A. R. Lawrence is a fine, strong,
albeit stagey feature in the entertainment. Parson
Adams, as faithfully reproduced by Mr. Brough,
is without question the one genuine article in
“Joseph’s Sweetheart’s ” hollow world, and a few
other people might be better or worse.
*• # * &
“ Paul Jones,” at Melbourne Opera House,
will last for one more week, whereas J. L. Toole
is to be brought to a conclusion at the Princess’
this (Thursday) evening. The bill consists of
“Paul Pry” and two of his best farces. On
Saturday a farewell fortnight of “Little Lord
Fauntleroy” will start, and all who desire to
slobber once again over that touching story of pure
and simple snobbery had better lose no time in
attending the pretty sickening spectacle.
■sic iK
“Silver King” further established its re-
putation as a noble story of how an insane indul-
gence in strong drink may drive a young man to
the possession of a mine of wealth untold, by fill-
ing Melbourne Royal on Saturday night. The
latest revival, of which much was expected, fell
about as flat as the play’s literary excellence will
permit it to fall under liberal management, and
this notwithstanding the pertinacity with whicli
Mr. Charles Warner came before the curtain and
bowed until scarcely the echo of a cheer remained
in the auditorium. In truth his performance of
Wilfrid Denver serves to alleviate the sorrow
caused by Charles’ approaching departure
to fill a long felt want at Drury Lane where
the sobbing of a strong man in his agony
doesn’t become wearisome by repetition in a rapid
sequence of characters. Wilfred Warner is too
all-pervading entirely. Ho gets too drunk to start
with, and then he takes far too long to sober up.
He repents a lot more than is good for him, and
endures a more protracted agony of remorse,
terminating in a more hysterical outbreak of joy,
than the case seems to require. Nevertheless,
Warner has his great moments—as at the end of
the third act—and then the prevailing desire to
see him buried with military honours tones down
into a feeling of admiration, mingled with
surprise that he should behave as he
does behave at other times. Miss Grade
Warner does well, indeed, as Nellie Denver, so
well that her chronic weakness at the chest seems
a matter for genuine regret. Royce again proves
that he is worthy to be bracketted with Phil Day
as an exponent of the fossil Jaikes, but the rest of
the show will be judged by the past and found
badly wanting. Taking the minor instance of
those three old gaffers at the roadside inn, who
have been steadily deteriorating in quality since
the drama was first played here nearly seven years
ago, those three men are now at their very worst.
“ Silver King ” is intended to run for the three
remaining weeks of Warner’s stay, and its vitality
should hold out that much.
* * *
“ Private Secretary ” is raging with in-
creased fury at the Alexandra, and the nightly
attendances to see Spalding flung into igno-
minious heaps grow in size and enthusiasm. The
Cogill Bros, have been reduced to the necessity of
jointly and severally filing their shovels, leaving
Harry Rickards to carry on the war at St. George’s
Hall for one more week. The new minstrel crowd
at the Victoria Hall turns out to be Horace Bent,
Whitburn, Franks and Marion and other memories
of the past, who have come out comparatively
fresh and are filled with earnest resolutions to do
the best they can.
** * *
At the concerts a tendency
to relapse into something distinctly suggesting an
air has resulted in a continuation of the season
for five more concerts. Last week The Bulletin
(having the second concert in its hard, practical
mind)intimatedthat vocal items were themainstays
of a dull entertainment. Next day the Age said “it
is for many reasons unfortunate that the manage-
ment should have thought it necessary to diversify
these concerts with vocal selections. The singing
of a modern ballad jars upon the ear,” &c. It so
jars upon the ear that the whole Town Hall rises
at the top notes of Charles Turner and Miss Mon-
tague, and encores them with a heartiness which
makes the false applause bestowed upon the
sonatas seem as ridiculous as the wretched cant of
daily paper drivellers.
** * *
At the Adelaide Royal, Wybert Reeve is
running a season, supported by Edith Blande,
Flora Hastings, James Stevenson and the most of
the late Bland Holt Company. That drama of
antiquity, “Green Bushes,” was the opening
piece, followed by “Lost in London,” and the
business has been fairly satisfactory. Arrange-
ments are being made to get “ After Dark ”
scenery and effects from Melbourne, and this
drama will probably be staged in the holy city ere
long. Harmston’s Circus is flourishing.
*# * *
Another theatre for Sydney ! The new
temple is to be erected on part of the Hill estate,
opposite Tattersall’s, in Pitt-street, and is de-
signed to eclipse even the Melbourne Princess,
Mr. W. T. Muston is to build the new structure,
and Mr, Clarence Backhouse ia the architect.
Work has already been been begun, and the cur-
tain will ring up for the first time about Christ-
mastide.
At Poverty Point.
A youthful Oobargo shepherd has been telling his flock
that nine-tenths of the novels in the local library are not
nt to read ; then he went on to condemn the surroundings
of the theatres—“the clerk who had just robbed his
master’s money-desk, the degraded and immoral man and
woman, the thief, the blackguard, the sharper, the
habitues of the gambling-saloon, were all mixed up in the
street outside,” &c. How did the reverend acquire this
information ? When he gets older he will learn, if he
reads the evening papers, that the elder who has em-
bezzled the trust-funds of the widow, and the parson who
has seduced a member of his Bible class, are usually not
found in the theatre—unless in disguise.
Bluntly says the S.F. Newsletter: “ Whatever may
be Mr. and Mrs. Kendal’s good qualities, they have, in
common, one vice which is most detestable to all Cali-
fornia, and that is—meanness. It is very much to be
doubted whetjier two actors ever came to San Francisco
who were so unwholesomely selfish, and so utterly dis-
liked by those who came in business contact with them.”
Mme. Patti and Mrs. “ Immaculate” Kendal, as some of
the Yankee papers call that lady, have come to wordy
war. Mme. Patti resented Mrs. Kendal’s claim to a super-
abundance of morality, and intimated that Mrs. Kendal
travelled more upon her virtue than on her art. Mrs.
Kendal, at latest, was “ real mad.”
Amy Horton, announced in London as “the leading
Australian burlesque actress,” travelled'part of the way
on the same boat as the talented eldest son of the Prince
of Wales, and she says “ he was very unaffected and
pleasant to everybody.” We daresay Amy meant it, but
we are afraid that, like most women, she would have been
pleased with a Future King if he had only said “ hee-
haw ! ” in a loud voice.
Much regret is felt in show circles at the insolvency of
Taylor, the circus man, as straightforward an employer as
ever lived. He has been associated with Burton, Bird,
and all the others.
Melbourne low comedians, dressed up as aboriginals,
tried to surprise Johnny Toole in a bush excursion, but
he didn’t come that way, and the aboriginals had their
clothes stolen, wherefore they got locked up.
J. P. Burnett, Jo’s Burnett, is about Melbourne picking
up health. He is a bit too thin, but will soon be ready for
the familiar Bucket.
Eighteen years ago a fund was being raised for the
benefit of a widow and orphans, and at one of the meetings
Coppin was asked to act as treasurer. “No !” said George
decisively, “it would injure the cause that we all have at
heart. It would be said that the movement was one of
1 Coppin’s dodges.’ ” And so Charley Cavanagh, of the
Theatre Royal, was appointed.
Donaldson Bros., contortionists, lately from Australia,
are a hit in London.
“The wife of Charles Oharrington of a daughter.”
Agnes Thomas, it seems, has made rather a large success
as Jane Shore, at the London Standard. In Melbourne
and Sydney she will long be remembered for her perform-
ance of Mrs. Ralston, in “Jim the Penman,” and of a
pretty light part in “A Night-off.” Why don’t B. and B.
get hold of “ A Night-off ” and reproduce it with Tither-
adge, Anson and Annie Taylor in their original characters?
Edward Thorne, of America, has seceded from the
Bland Holt company to travel with Van Tassell’s ballooning
crowd. Chichester, late of B. and B.’s, fills up the gap.
Lady Florence Dixie says that the cardinal sin of her
sex is the wear of long skirts. Her idea is that the skirt
should come no lower than the knee, and that that por-
tion of the female leg which is euphemistically described
as the ankle, should be made visible. The only serious
objection, probably, says a contemporary, would come
from ladies of the ballet, who might object, in view of such
unrestricted competition, but this would probably adjust
itself by the ladies of the histrionic profession outstripping
their sisters in private life. Lady Florence’s reform is
worthy of serious consideration, and if the sterner sex be
allowed to vote upon it, the result may be predicted in
advance.
Coppin’s father was a doctor, afterwards actor and
manager. George, though very young indeed, ere arrival
in Australia had considerable experience as a low
comedian in the provinces, and even at London minor
theatres. The toss-up of a penny decided him on coming
to Australia instead of America. His friend, Morton
King, tragedian, was afterwards a Melbourne auctioneer,
magistrate, and member of Parliament.
G. B. W. Lewis still flourishes in Melbourne as hearty
as ever. He began life as an acrobat (some will remem-
ber his Risley act, with Lilliputian Tom, in Rowe’s
Circus, 36 years ago), and the writer recollects him at
Ooppin’s Old English Pair, Cremorne Gardens, Lewis in
puce-coloured acrobatic tights, at the bar, taking
lemonade (“ Not me,” says G. B. W.—yes, ’twas). After
that, though well on in years, he married Rose Edouin,
and they have a grown-up family, with more money made
in Bombay than in Melbourne.
James Smith tells us that G. H. Rogers, the come-
dian, was a brother of Professor Henry Rogers, the
distinguished writer. Charley Young’s first Melbourne
appearance was as Claude Melnotte. Rogers and his wife,
both talented, at one time only got £2 10s a week between
them.
Mrs. Brough is going to make a great thing out of the
study of the part of Clarissa Harlowe, Buchanan’s new
Vaudeville comedy, Winifred Emery’s success in London.
Winifred was in Australia with her father, Sam.
During Fred. Leslie’s illness his part in the “ Blase
Routi ” was played by Fred. Story with much acceptation.
Poor Prank Stephenson who, wasted by consumption,
came to Australia for his health, died in Melbourne on
Friday, 30th May. His performance of Giro Panitza, in
“My Jack,” was a very powerful one, and will not soon be
forgotten by Sydney Her Majesty’s and Melbourne Alex-
andra audiences.
The Henry Bracy English Opera Co. has been doing
good business in Brisbane with the “Beggar Student”
and the “ Sultan of Mocha.”
Arbitrator Bancroft has decided the Langtry-Haddon
Chambers case re the failure to produce “The Bouquet,”
in favour of the author with “ exemplary damages.”
Rev. E. W. Gulliver, who comes from New Zealand with
first-class credentials as a liberal-minded man and a fluent
lecturer, takes the Sydney platform shortly.
A Chicago paper says that Charles Arnold will never be
considered the star of his company while he has the
diminutive comedienne, May Hannan, in the organization.
Kate Vaughan has burnt her eyelids by the incautious
use of her smelling-bottle.
The roof of a theatre in which Madame Janauschek was
performing recently blew off, but the audience were so
spell-bound that they never noticed it. So her manager
says, and he ought to know.
At the conclusion of the mad-scene in Lucia, Patti
stepped down to the footlights in response to an ovation
and sang “Home, Sweet Home,” at N.Y. Metropolitan.
Talk of the artistic proprieties !
When Barnum goodbyed a British circus-manager, the
latter remarked ; “ We shall meet in Heaven, I trust, my
dear Barnum.” “ Yes,” said Barnum thoughtfully, “if
you’re there.”
Sculptor Sala, of Cow Plat, N.S.W., has accidently un-
earthed another marble man, weighing 5001b5., from the
Oaleula quarries. The figure is in a crouching position,
and it is alleged that when discovered it was sitting on a
horse in close conversation with a whale, the subject of
discussion being Jonah’s ejection from several places of
entertainment while “ padding the hoof ” to Tarshish.
Salt Lake criticism; “An alleged actor named Band-
mann gave a very rooky performance of ‘ The Merchant
of Venice ’ in Salt Lake Theatre Monday evening, before
an audience of 100 people. The theatregoers of this city,
however, well knowing Bandmann to be a ten-cent actor,
preferred to witness the acting of the heifer in ‘ Evange-
line ’at the Grand, which was crowded. Anyone who has
seen the acting of Evangeline’s heifer and compared it
with that of Bandmann, will unhesitatingly say that the
heifer is much the better actor.”
Marian Burton and Nellie Stewart are to alternate
Boccaccio at the Melb. Opera House.
The Baimsfathers, when on the way to Melbourne from
Puget Sound, in the “ Flying Venus,” were wrecked on
Penrhyn Island in the Pacific, and lost all their possessions
excepting their top notes. Some of the sailors made
Samoa in an open boat, and from thence the S.S. Bion-
mond was despatched to rescue the castaways after
sojourning for forty-seven days in the vicinity of some
simple but cavernous savages.
R. A. Aginton is now skating at London Olympia.
Mat Berry, so well-known and liked when he visited
Australia with Katie Putnam, has been laid up with
pneumonia in Salt Lake City.
W. G. Eaton, chef d’orchestre, lately in Australia, has
written to a London paper insinuating thatpersons offered
engagements in Australia cannot be too careful as to the
framing of their contracts.
Joseph K. Emmet (“ Fritz”) has been served with
notice of divorce-suit by Eleanor, his wife, on grounds of
alleged adultery with Maud White, his leading lady, and
others. Young Joseph, who manages, has biffed the lady
out of the company, and she sues for breach of contract.
Emmet denies unfaithfulness.
Boston Ideal Co. have been working down from Brisbane
towards Sydney, whence it is to be presumed they turn
Amerioa-wards.
Carmenoita, the wonderfully graceful Spanish dancer,
whose philosophic views on the alluring of public atten-
tion by mere suggestiveness instead of vulgar disclosure
have so largely helped to make her fortune, is making an
increased sensation in New York. The Herald, the Sun,
and the World, have lately devoted from two to six
columns in a Sunday’s issue to her ! and for days previ-
ously the World advertised that its Sunday edition would
contain “ two large-sized views of Oarmencita’s slipper.”
Should her feet be as sole-full as those of an American
actress now in Australia, the World will require to stretch
a point to get both in one page.
After nine years’ absence Emily Soldene has appeared at
Frisco Tivoli in “The Drum Major’s Daughter.”
J. L. Toole showed at Albury on his way over on Friday
night.
D’Arcy Stanfield’s crowd and the Taylor-Oarrington
combination are having a rough time with the N.Q. floods.
It is denied that the Kendals are about to built a
theatre in New York. An American writer states that
Mrs. K.’s smug hypocritical affectation of super-theatrical
purity has alienated from her the affection of all other
performers.
Jennie Lee will shortly produce “ Run Wild,” a play of
which she has purchased the Australian rights from Willie
Edouin. The heroine is a warm-hearted girl, full of mis-
chief, and the incidental business is likely to suit the little
woman.
A. H. Gee, an amateur baritone well known in Sydney,
will make his first appearance on the operatic stage in
“ Boccaccio.” Tenor Kennedy, of “ Twa hours at hame ”
fame, also joins the Nellie Stewart Co.
J. R. Rogers and Minnie Palmer have been thrown from a
carriage at Manchester (Eng.). Minnie was badly bruised;
John had his leg broken. Good and inexpensive “ad.”
for “ yours merrily.”
The Victorian Orchestra management have appealed to
the Melbourne City Council for “ concessions ” in the
matter of the rent paid for using the Town Hall. The
appeal was referred to the Public Works Committee. The
orchestra was never at any time a “ public ” work.
A project is on foot to import to Melbourne ere Cup
week a large party of military musicians picked from the
crack English regimentffl bands. Preliminaries are now
being arranged, and the venture will be managed by a
well-known firm of entrepreneurs.
Bernhardt has been rehearsing for Cleopatra with a live
snake.
“ Niblo” (Thomas Clarke), the once-famous trapezist, is
dead. He wa» styled “Niblo” by a manager who had
a lot of single-letter Btbndin posters on hand, and, wish-
ing to use them, manufactured the name.

presented to the veteran critic, Doctor James Edward

Hambleton, the favourite bush comedian of Melbourne,
in very old times, is now a preacher.
George Darrell has been unhusking some hoary old
hickory-nuts at Ballarat with great success under Mac-
Mahonian management.
It is said that Wallace, who composed “Maritana" in
Sydney, married a Maori girl named Tatea in New Zealand.
Bay, comedian of the old Queen’s Theatre, Melbourne,
turned it up, went shepherding in the bush, and got so
absorbed reciting that the dingoes killed 37 of his sheep.
He was fined £l2O, but hadn’t a cent. Went to chokey.
Recited there, and got let loose on the world again.
A young lieutenant, son of the great tragedian
Macready, now serves with the English army in India.
This is explained by Macready having, at about 75 years
of age—like Premier Parkes —married a young lady, his
s eoond wife. Mrs. Macready still lives, and is likely to
live another generation.
The late Sir Win. Stawell was a poor,
meagre-looking, whiffling young man when he
started at the Bar. Once he had to defend a hulk-
ing fellow, and said to the jury, “ Regard this
miserable creature in the dock.” The prisoner
put in “Miserable yourself ! Who are you calling
miserable ?”
An English writer cleverly says of “Miss
J.,” the young woman who pursued the Duke of
Wellington with an eye to his conversion and his
coronet, that she “ appears to have been one of
those persons, not very uncommon, and occa-
sionally met with by most men during their lives,
with a deep and false sense of religion, and no
conscience whatever.”
It is related of the present Duchess of
Marlbrough that one of her old escapades was when
out driving with two young men, one on either
side of her. During the drive, finding two hands
slipped ingratiatingly into her muff, where they
felt about for her own, she quietly withdrew her
fingers and thrust them under the rug. The two
hands came in contact, grasped each other and
squeezed lovingly. The two young men looked
vacantly at the horse’s ears and were filled with
inward joy. The future duchess, with her hands
warmly folded under the rug, also looked at the
horse’s ears with a faint sphinx-like smile.
The 45th annual report of the New York
Life Insurance Company, published in our
columns to-day, is full of eloquent figures showing
wonderful achievements during 1889. A total in-
come of £6,000,672 Is. 3d. means an increase in
income over 1888 of £774,070 13s. 2d. Paid to
policy-holders £2,494,057 19s, Id.; and total
assets, January 1, 1890, of £21,615,967 ss. 6d.;
increase in assets, £2,381,360 19s. 9d. Number of
policies issued during the year 39,499, insuring
£31,094,463. The New York Life is a purely
mutual company, with investments of the most
productive character, and has made greater per-
manent advances than any other company, while
care in the selection of lives has given the
company a more favourable mortality than
other companies. The New York Life was
the first company to recognise the policy-
holder’s right to paid-up insurance, in case of a
discontinuance of premiums, by originating and
introducing, in 1860, the first non-forfeiture
policies. This Company also was the first, and
for 35 years the only Company, to omit from its
policies the clause making them void in case of
suicide. This Company issues a greater variety of
policies than any other company, and the results
of its tontine investment policies have, not been
equalled by any other company. No useless re-
strictions upon residence, travel, or occupation.
This Company is domiciled in Australia, pays all
claims at the neai’est branch-office, and is in every
way amenable to. the jurisdiction of the colonial
courts.

The Rum ford Plains Tragedy.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
I.
Statement made bv Gilbert Vaughan, Manager
oe the L.S.D. Bank, Wattleville.
It was a serious difficulty, and had overtaken
me so suddenly that my presence of mind entirely
forsook me, and I saw no way out of it except by
instant escape. There was the dead body, slain
by my hand, and in a few moments I should be
confronted with the girl whom I had intended to
ask to be my wife that morning. How was Ito
face her, knowing how fondly she had loved the
poor victim ?
The act had been quite unintentional; although
there had never been much love lost between us,
I had never meant liis death. It had been simply
the fault of hasty temper on my side and un-
fortunate curiosity on his. I had ridden over that
morning with my heart filled with the gentlest
feelings ; the bright morning and sunny landscape
seemed to whisper nothing but peace, and now,
by an inconsiderate blow, I had dispelled all my
h ipes, and saw no escape but in instant and imme-
diate flight. To continue standing by the poor
corpse was the act of an idiot. By a strange
chance no one was about; I had ridden up quite
unperceived. So I mounted my horse and hastened
back to the township I had left that morning with
such different feelings.
My duties at the bank that day (I was the
manager of a small country branch) were, fortu-
nately for me, of the slightest, for my mind was
constantly running on that morning’s tragedy, and
X was ceaselessly wondering if my deed had yet
been discovered, and picturing the sorx-ow of the
innocent girl whom I loved so fondly. At thre e
0 clock I heard a voice in the bank asking the
teller if I was in, and soon afterwards, to my
amazement, Ah Foo, the Chinese cook at Rumford
Plains, walked into the small apartment that
served as manager’s room.
As he glanced at me with his cunning almond
eyes I saw in a m®ment that my secret was
known, and it did not need that he should take
out two small objects and place them on the table
to confirm this suspicion. For an instant I had
wild thoughts of shooting him down with the
bank revolver and swearing that he tried to stick
up the place, but I restrained myself in order to
hear what he had to say.
" I saw you kill him, Misser Yawn, and I welly
glad. No fear I say anything. Evelybody ask ;
Ino savee. Fvelybody say Misser Muspius; I
no savee, only laugh. Missee Lawrence she Gy,
dy, all day. Think it Misser Muspius doee.”
‘‘Ah Xoo, I said, ‘‘you’re a brick; here’s a
sovereign for you. ”
‘ Allight, Misser Yawn. I no savee who kill
him, only, when evelybody say, Misser Muspius’
I laugh,” and he laughed himself out of the room
only to reappear for an instant. “You go, see
Missee Lawrence to-night ?” he whispered in a
stage aside, and vanished.
O i course I would. I would make the most of
the golden opportunity. Muspius, my hated
rival, was evidently suspected, and Ah Foo had
slyly confirmed these suspicions. I was safe, so
long as I could bribe Ah Foo; at any rate I would
take his advice and go over to Rumford Plains a
once-it was only five miles, and I would take
care that the suspicions thrown on Muspius should
be confirmed. I had taken the first step in crime ;
the second was not at all hard.
11.
Statement Made bv John Muspius, Superin-
tendent of Mekridale Station.
It was a pure accident, but a most unfortunate
one, to happen on the very morning when I rode
over to Rumford Plains to propose to Miss
Lawrence. Just as I was going to hang my horse
up I saw Tommy standing at the low fence, with
his head over the second rail, watching me. Now,
I had had more than one bridle broken through
his tricks, and after ineffectually telling him
several times to clear out, I gave him a tap with
the double of my whip. It caught him on the
back of the neck, and, to my astonishment, he
dropped down dead. It struck me at once that
no one would believe it was an accident, for onlv
the other evening I had got into a dispute with
Lawrence about shooting blacks in North Queens-
land ; and he had said that ho would not trust
anyone’s life in my hands. Of course he was in a
temper because I had the best of the argument,
but this acoidmt happening just after such a
remark would look altogether too suspicious ; and
besides, I dared not face Miss Lawrence, for I knew
how fond she was of Tommy. There was no one
about, so I just rode quietly off and into Wattle-
ville to think it over.
About half-past two that afternoon old Jennings
the landlord of the Royal, told me that Ah Foo’
the cook at Rumford Plains, wanted to see me.’
Well, Ah Foo, ’ I said, when the old scoundrel
came in, ‘what do you want?” I had no sus-
ui mou at the that he had witnessed the ui,
happy affair. He grinned- and made a motion
\vi. . .iis arm like sinking a blow, that at once
proved to me he knew all. “ Welly unlucky
Misser Muspius,” he said, “ poor Tommy—dead.’’
Ah ! I said ; it cap tbe helped. You know
I never meant to kill him.”
“ I savee,” he replied, “ I saw you. Everyone
r.ay Misser Yawn kill Tommy. I no savee, only
augh. Missee Lawrence cly, cly, cly.” So that
confounded bank jaukeroo, Vaughan, was sus-
pected, was he ? Well, the very best thing that
could happen. I gave Ah Poo a Sovereign, and he
winked and Said, “ You go see Missee Lawrence,
I think welly good.” Then he vanished. Under
the circum tances this was excellent advice, and I
determined to follow it. Of cour. e I would not
go out of my way to shift the blame on to
Vaughan, but if anything was said about the
matter I would not hide my opinion of him. All’s
fair in love and war. Besides, he had no business
to be out there at that time in the morning ;
serve him right if it was the means of getting him
into trouble.
111.
Extract from the Diary of Miss Selina
Lawrence.
May Ist. —Such an unhappy commencement to
the day ; I never thought I should feel so glad
afterwards as Ido now. About 11 o'clock papa
came to me to say that poor Tommy was dead—
killed, seemingly, by a blow on the back of the
neck! I almost fainted when I heard it.
The men were all away at the yards, and no
strangers had been seen about the place. Poor
Tommy; 1 cried bitterly all the morning. His
body was laid out and I put some flowers on hint?
he was such a good-hearted, faithful fellow. Papa
is very indignant and says he will never rest
until the guilty party is found out; I never saw
him so rou ed before. He says it is a most
abominable crime to be committed in broad day.
While I was still sorrowing over poor Tommy's
fate the mail arrived. Such glorious news ! A
letter from Fred saying that h's uncle lias retired
and handed his practice over to him; so now
there’s no reason why we can’t get married at
once and bring our long engagement to an end—so
he writes. Papa’s very pleased, too; he says that
the practice is worth nearly two thousand a year,
and we are actually going to start to Sydney to-
morrow morning, so I’m tired out packing up.
Mr. Vaughan and Mr. Muspius came over this
evening. They both seemed so absent-minded
df] £ j
CL cl’ ,
and jealous of each other. I suppose, papa told
them what had h ippened when they went out on
the verandah to smoke, for they both, I am glad
to say, went away early.
Poor Tommy! this good news put his death
almost clean out of my head for the moment.
IV.
Statement of Ah Foo, Cook at Rumford
Plains.
(Translated into ordinary/ Enr/Hsh.)
I remember May Ist. I was looking out of tlm
kitchen window when I saw Mr. Vaughan ride
up. Just as he was going up to the house. Tommy,
Miss Lawrence’s pet emu, came up and pecked
at the buckles on his saddle-pouch, and his horse
started back and broke h : s bridle. Mr. Vaughan
turned back and caught his horse and when Tommy
came up again, he hit him with the butt-end (if
his whip on the back of his neck, and knocked
him down. After looking at him for a minute, In-
got on his horse again and rode back to town. I
went out to see if Tommy was dead, and as he
was not quite, I finished him, for he was always
in mischief. Just then I saw Mr. Muspius coming
so I put Tommy up against the fence with his head
through, to hold him up, and went back into the
kitchen. Mr. Muspius looked round when he got
off and saw Tommy, so he gave him a flick with
the double of the stockwhip he was carrying, and
Tommy tumbled down. He thought he’d killed
him, for he got on his horse again and rode away
just the same as Air. Vaughan. I put poor Tommy
up again with his head through the fence, and
just then Mr. Lawrence came along. “There’s
that d emu,” he said, “trying to get into
the garden and he picked up a stick and threw
it at him, and down went poor Tommy. I came
out and looked at him and he looked at me. “My
word,” I said, “Missee Lawrence make a fuss.”
“Hush,” he said,” you no savee anything ; ’ and
he gave me a pound—and he went in and tell
Missee some bominable wletcli killed Tommy.
That afternoon I went in to Wattleville, and
Mr. Muspius gave me a pound not to tell, and Mr.
Vaughan gave me another. Then, in the even-
ing, Missee Lawrence came into the kitchen and
said : “Ah Poo, Im going to Sydney to-morrow
morning to get married. Here's a pound to bury
poor Tommy properly.”
Next morning young Wilson, the new-chum,
from the next station, came over, and lie said,
when he saw Tommy : “ Ah Poo, I want an emu-
skin to send home to England, to say I shot him.
You skin me this nicely and I’ll give you a pound. ’
That velly good emu, that makee me hive
pounds.
DELCOMYN.
Dear Bulletin, —I read that a drunken
man, in Melbourne, lay out in the open, speech-
lessly intoxicated, for a whole night, and that,
when ultimately run in, the sum of £lO was found
in his pockets. That reminds me of how my
gentlemanly friend Trooper Jack M , once
a dashing officer in the N.S.W. constabulary,
came to leave the force. Fancy it is M— who
is speaking, and this is how he.»old it me : —I was
stationed in a one-horse township where the only
other policeman was the lock-up keeper, a married
man. One day I was out on my one-street beat
and I noticed, lying outside a pub. door, a filthy
heap of drunken raggery, too dirty almost to
touch with a pitchfork. I sauntered past, super-
ciliously—a way we have in the foorce. I passed
him a dozen times, perhaps, but let him snore
away unheeded. Presently the landlord came out
and asked me, as a personal favour, to
hump the loafer along to the lockup. I
looked at the heap of blistering humanity and
thought vaguely of insecticide, disinfectants and
so on, but, as we bobbies had to keep sweet with
publicans, it behoved me to pocket my disgust and
run in the unfortunate psoriasist; so I engaged
two blackfellows to carry the beggar along to the
Jogs. Arrived there, the keeper’s wife entered
the charge on the sheet, as the keeper himself was
a-bed- with “ fever-’n-aiger.” I let the helpless
tatterdemalion fall backwards into the cell, and
was about to lock the cell-door, when Mrs. Keeper
renarked —“I suppose we ought to search him
and take everything from him?” replied I—“ I
don't like to touch him just now. He’s a
mere drunken loafer and hasn't got any-
thing, I expect. ” Mrs. Keeper, however, insisted,
and as lovely woman's wish was always
my weakness, I gingerly “went through him,”
gave him a rat-shake, and 10, something fell out
from somewhere. I looked at Mrs. Keeper. She
looked coldly and matter-of-fact like at me, and
merely said, “Count it.” I did. It was all in
notes —£397 sterling.” That woman entered it
down as if it were a pair of socks in a washing-
bill. Then she locked up the loot and resumed
her “ official ” station, I went outside to curse.
Bringing my conduct under my own mental re-
view, I was so disgusted that I discharged myself
from the force that night—i.e., I wrote out my
resignation. My verdict was; “Jack M ,
you’re too soft an idiot to adorn the police. Here’s
the very chance you’ve been waiting for, and you
never smelt it under your nose, nor saw it beneath
your eyes. £397 sterling ! Go in mourning. Jack,
and resign at once.” And I did. Yours, The D.

Rebuked for having dropped a shirt-button
into the offertorial bag, lie inquired loudly ;

A certain Italian scientist, who is an
enthusiastic collector of ethnological specimens,
has been compelled, on arrival at Port Moresby
to return certain specimens taken by force from
the New Guinea natives. This incident, in which
there may be a spice of national jealousy, has
been quoted by sundry papers in justification of
the justice of British rule.

BEERY
The ruling nations of the world drink beer—the
tough, stubborn Briton drinks ale and beer —the
strong, hardy German drinks beer, and the
American is beginning to drink more beer than
anything else. The Australian drinks St. Louis
Lager Beer, because there is no drink in the world
so conducive to social intercourse. It gently
moves the blood to accelerated action and arouses
the generous sentiments that cluster around the
human heart.

pointments were made, the British Army boasted
of five Field Marshals. These included Gamp-
ridge, Tummy, and Lord Napier. The latter is
already dead, and the others have never been
alive, in a military sense.

Worthy Alfred Woolley, who died in
Melbourne the other day, was the first man (in
conjunction with partner Campbell) to put whisky
upon the Victorian market. It is nearly fifty
years ago since the evergreen James Cowie im-
ported the first shipment to Geelong of a liquor
which was then little known outside of Scotland.
He sold it to Woolley and Campbell, and a fine
taste for Mountain Dhu began to make itself felt
from that time forward. That was the real
genuine usher whisky.
An article we have just read upon the old
Cheshire Cheese Tavern, in Fleet-street, London,
speaks of “ Congreve’s wit flashing as he sat with
legs comfortably stretched under the tables, as
Pope, Goldsmith, Chatterton and Garrick came
and went. ” Considering that Chatterton, a boy
of seventeen, was only in London for five months,
during most of which time he was starving, it is
difficult to believe that the infant phenomenon
came to the Cheshire Cheese) unless it was to
potter around after broken victuals.

Victoria is more Hinglish than Hingland.
Her citizens are eternally protesting their loyalty
to “ the Throne and Sovereign,” her papers
‘■gush” more over the same fetishes than do
their English comtemporaries, and her churches
and halls resound with exhortations to “be loyal.”
“Ain’t it sick’nin?”
Most French Courts of Justice have either
a painting of the Crucifixion, or a large Crucifix,
on the wall behind the judge or magistrate. The
Minister of Justice and political Minister of
Religion refuse to take them down because
“ people can take them just as they please.” For
agnostical cynicism one must go to France.
•Stead says that the circulation of the
Tunes is only 25,000, and that it will be shortly
reduced in price to a penny. It is at the shrines
of such played-out, unreal gods as this that we are
asked to worship. The once-dreaded fulminations
of the clergy now appeal only to the old women of
both sexes. When the Tunis “ thunders,” people
now hear not heavy artillery, but only the tom-
tom of political superstition.
Varney Parkes, son of the Premier of that
ilk, proposes to re-enter political life Unless Var-
ney can invent an improved Kiarna Ghost or some
little tiling like that, lie had better stick to archi-
tecture, for, to judge by ids legislative perform-
ances up to date, he will always be known as Sir
’Enary’s son, which, when you come to look at it
dispassionately and all round and inside-out, will
b.) a tremendous obstacle in the way of his doing
anything original.
The peroration of the maiden speecli of
Mr. Walter Hussey Vivian, a newly - elected
member of the N.S.W. Parliament, is too good to
be entombed in the, dark grave of Hansard :
it is only by comparison that we can test the relative
m wits of different count l ies, and as I survey in my mind's
eye tile whole of the civilised world, I find that if we con-
centrate all the countries into one great pyramid, the
apex is crowned by New South Wales shedding the light
of her glory, her genius, and her wealth over this vast
ocean of humanity.
“ There are but two possible solutions of
the labour problem : either capital must own the
labour or labour must own the capital. The first
means chattel slavery ; the second national co-
operation. All attempts to settle the question
which include the retention of the competitive
system must, in the very nature of things, fail.
The new wine cannot be kept in old bottles. The
world must either advance into nationalism or
v,.f. H do into slavery.”— Journal of Unitkd
Labour.
- •!>• Czar coolly*announced last week that
Sebastopol i, s again to become purely a military
and naval port, all the commerce being trans-
ferred to another place. And so goes the last
shred uf all England fought for in the Crimea.
Put England really-went to war to establish the
French Usurper, as a catspaw, and failed to do it.
Behind this was the hatred of Victoria and Albei t
to Republicanism. Even the Royalist Kuiglake
exposes the false issues, and the Times is now
punished for hounding on the war.
'I he N.S.W. Civil Service Inquiry Com-
mission still nominally lives, notwithstanding that
its recommendations have been, with but few
exceptions, quickly shelved by the Government.
Messrs. M‘Millau and Whitson could not see their
way clear to continue their work on those terms,
s>» fell out of the running; and now Janies
Thomson lias thrown up the sponge. “Tom”
Bibbs, however, means to stick to the wreck
whilst two planks hang together, and lie
punctually draws his nine little guineas a week.
Abigail, M.P. (N.S.W.), now in England,
denies that the exclusion of the Chinese from
Australia was due to the Protectionists-! Tim
movement was purely a Protectionist one, was
initiated by Protectionist papers and politicians,
and was (this we say on the authority of Mr.
Gladstone, the greatest living Freetrader) in direct
opposition to the doctrines of Cobdenism. The
agitation, until it became irresistible, was but
half-heartedly supported by the Foreign-trade
press, and was only taken up by Parkes to dish
his political opponents. Why should the Foreign-
trader, who imported nigger compositors with
skeleton fingers and poor-turkey legs, suddenly
draw the line at Chinamen?
“Uncle ’ Abigail, M.P., whose “fiscal”
letters to the Times are, let us hope, carefully
revised ere being fired at Buckle, is also attending
to the “ popery ” plank of his political platform in
promoting the visit to Australia of red-hot Orange
lecturers to uphold the Union. Now that the
N.S.W. Party Processions Act has been prac-
tically repealed through the cowardice of a Par-
liament which shrinks from incurring the censure
of the parti-coloured vermin, is just the time for
King Billy’s brigade to invade this country with
reinforcements and bite publicly the ears off the
Pope’s gang. The Bulletin hopes that the
Gatling gun will never be used by any Australian
Government save on the day when the yellow and
green indulge in a faction-fight.
S. E. Lees, M L.A., in arguing for the
repeal of the N.S.W. Party Processions (i.e., man-
slaughter discouragement) Act, pointed out that
the Salvation Army processions paraded the
street and yet no ill-feeling was created—the days
of intolerance were gone. Lees’ claptrap deceives
no one —not even himself. The Salvation Army
makes a big noise, but it tries to inculcate the love
of fellow man, and certainly does a lot of good
in the world to balance its output of religious
mania, whereas the Orange organisation, without
which a lot of prosy geysers would probably not
have the privilege of boring Parliament with their
—er —Municipal-Council English, teaches yon to
love Mr. Sammy Lees, and Uncle Abigail, and
’Ezlot and ’Enson, and all the rest of the hopeless
drones whose nnreportable commonplaces are the
joy of the newspaper stenographers. Most of the
Salvation Army godliness is artless and uncalcu-
lating, whereas Orange piety is beastly deliberate,
and too often the foundation of a seat in Parlia-
ment.

THE RABBIT NUISANCE.
In the ,A ssem bly yesterday afternoon, My.
Hassall drew attention to the urgent necessity
fo r fresh legislation to deal with the rabbit
pesf The bon member emphasised the fact that
something should be dene atonce, otherwise the
pest weald spread to such aa extent that it
would become an extremely difficult matter to
do anything with it at all. To the people
settled oat in the Western districts it had be-
come a matter more important than the land
question, more important than the fiscal question
and more important even than the question
of Federation.

laughter the Clerk read the document, which

of the district of Bnngawalbin, consider it an

against the ravages of vermin which intent this
district.

Society
People of sensitive and delicate organisa-
tions are advised to avoid the attached paragraph
as they would a serpent. It was perpetrated by
one Charles Marrson, a reverend pastor at Glenelg
(South Australia), and many moral citizens who
have attempted to read it have already faded
away. Listen: —
Allow me to protest against the dirty-minded and in-
solent people who would, under plea ol advocating Gospel
temperance, aflix the brand of harlotry upon those
engaged in one branch of the Christian ministry, our
barmaids. Those hard-working and long-suffering girls,
who truly serve God in their vocation and ministry,
deserve more chivalrous treatment than they receive at
the bands of their reviling accusers. If they have extra
temptations they should receive extra protection,
and not slander and defamation, from those who
care for the beautiful life. 1 indignantly deny that
the vocation of barmaid is generally inconsistent with
chastity. I have many friends among those so engaged,
and honestly believe their profession contains no greater
proportion of lewd women than any other trade. If bar-
maids are exposed to rude and profane language their
virtues are certainly robust enough not to go into a
galloping consumption thereupon, but the rude and pro-
fane language they hear in bars is certainly no ruder or
profaner than they would hear from the smug and sancti-
monious professors of so-called Gospel temperance. 1
can only recommend those unfortunate slanderers to go
regularly to the bar, and drink of the excellent wines of
the colony, and procure the honour of an acquaintance
with a large number of the girls who wait there, and lints
get their unamiable delusions dispelled.
Ahem! “ One branch of the Christian ministry !”
“Truly serve God ”at the beer-pump! Let the.
Church and the Young Men’s Christian Associa-
tion grasp some solid object for support as it reads
these awful words. The reverend teacher “has
many friends ” among barmaids. Also, he calls
his colleagues “ smug and sanctimonious pro-
fessors. ” What a falling-off is here ! And he
wants his congregation to go and liquor up
“regularly.” Great Jones! How regularly, we
would like to know ? And on top of all the other
disasters he asks them to become acquainted with
“a large number” of barmaids —not merely sixteen
Dr so, but a large number—quite irrespective of
Ivhat a fellow’s wife or his best girl would say.
A song :
The Armidale (N.S.W.) bachelors think
that every man should have at least two girls. They
gave a ball the other day, at which were present oO
gentlemen—that is to say, 50 bachelors- and 100
ladies, some of which latter, after coining7o miles to
attend the festivities according to invitation,
were left to sit along the wall. This idea of haying
a wide choice of partners is new to polite society
—the bachelors did not invite anyone engaged in
“ trade ’’—and is apparently of Mormomc origin.

An alderman named Love, who may be
jealous, objects to the doctors at a northern
N.S.W. hospital calling the nurses by their Chris-
tian names. If there is not love between the hos-
pital doctors and the hospital nurses there is war.
Which is pretty nearly true of all men and women
brought into immediate contact with one another.

The maids at Government House, Mel-
bourne, are always suffering from some complaint,
or other. The typhoid epidemic was followed by
an influenza outbreak, and one morning last week
a still more dreadful outbreak put the whole
household, from Hopetoun downwards, in a
twitter, The wail of early infancy was heard
proceeding from elsewhere than the vice-regal
nursery. Enquiries into this mystery resulted in
the discovery, says rumour, that one of the resi-
dent maids had been suddenly seized with a live
bab y- *

# * *
Down at Hawthorne (Melb.) a certain
Revd. Angus has started a marriage business
which only requires judicious advertising to make
it play old gooseberry with Nat. Kinsman’s trade.
is fixed up Coffee P&l&ce, tind a biuss
plate upon one of the portals announces that the
resident shepherd is prepared to celebrate nuptials
at all hours, terms strictly moderate. The advan-
tages offered by the new system are obvious.
Married couples have of late developed a taste for
beginning wedded life in coffee-palaces (the bride s
m jther being careful to point out that the happy
man runs little risk of getting drunk upon the
premises), and here may Edwin and Angelina,
after being married in number 20 room, enjoy all
the delight of a honeymoon trip by simply dump-
ing down their carpet-bags in the chamber adjoin-
ing Kinsman certainly sells h s clients useful
articles of furniture at the little shop in Fitzroy,
but the inconvenience of one flesh taking up their
bed and walking forth into the cruel world is con-
siderable. Better engage furnished apartments
at the Coffee Palace, where the officiating parson
can see his young friends fairly started in their
new life, and breathe oily blessings on their union
through the keyhole.

Is “Redan” Massey, now in Australia,
identical with Lord Clarina, or is ho not? We
don’t worry much about peerage matters and lesuv e
the question to be decided by the well-known Mr.
C of Sydney, who, when he heard two squatters
talking about “ the Duke of Durham,” promptly
chipwd in * Ya-a-s-Duke great friend of our
family —sort of relation of ours in a way (the fact
being that there is no such person as the Duke of
Durham). “Ah,” said one of the squatters to the
snobbish know-all, “ we are talking about a stud-
bull—your uncle, probably.”
# # e
Sandhurst pietists complain of the militia
drill on Sunday. Have they forgotten that the
glorious Battle of Waterloo was fought on a Sun-
day, when the Lord of Battles so smiled on “our
arms ? And surely if we may act on a Sunday, we
may rehearse.
# # *
The proprietor of Gundagai (N.S.W.)
Times got married the other day and published a
full account of the proceedings and a list of the
gifts, including a pickle-fork and butter-knife, a
silver fish-slice, a terra-cotta jam-barrel, and some
drawn-thread pillow-slips—by way of a leading
article. The Gundagai Times is a lucky paper,
and its new proprietor has made a good start.
The Times’ last owner, Mr. El worthy —father of
the present owner—now in Heaven, left behind
him no less than five thousand pounds sterling-a
thing never previously heard of in connection with
any N.S.W. country paper.

Spurgeon, in Richmond, Melbourne, for a fashion-

* * *
On 24th May Governor Ilopetoun went
out to a vacant allotment near the sea to review
the troops, and a Bui.i.etin man went out to
review Hopctouh, who, under plumes that drooped
over him like a wattle, was almost invisible. He
looked like the pet of the family in his grand-
father’s regimentals. When his funereal-looking
u charger ” commenced to execute a Maypole
dance round the British flag the crowd laughed fit
to seriously injure themselves internally, and
when, On leaving parade, an ironical cheer set the
vice-regal war-horse prancing on his near side-legs,
Imperialism clenched its thin, white lips ami
looked ready to give up the game as not good
enough.

# # *
An Adelaide paper says that a leading
Adelaide grievance is “the open manner in which
the local cabmen are permitted to suggest im-
moral journeys in their vehicles.” If we hadn’t
read anything about Adelaide in the evening
press, we’d have thought that the holy city was a
place in which the cabman’s leading question was
“ Shall I drive you to choir-practice, sir ?”
# # *
Up North a doctor sued a friendly society
for professional attendance. Three witnesses
swore that plaintiff was generally drunk and unfit
to enter anyone’s house Plaintiff replied that he
could always attend to his business however drunk
he was, and put in his written agreement with the
lodge and got a verdict. Drunken doctors must
be ranked, with scrub-fever, alligators, and myall
blacks, among the curses of the Far North.
* * *
The Edison phonograph was lately used in
America for the purpose of enabling a preacher to
read his own funeral-sermon, after death—not-
withstanding that the cynical public, none too
anxious to listen to his words of comfort and
wisdom while he was alive, absolutely didn t want
to hear any more about him after his skedaddlement.
The Bulletin would suggest that the phonograph
be henceforth used to enable the absent bride-
groom to say at the wedding-breakfast that this
is the happiest day of my life also, that Baronet
Clarke’s speeches at Masonic banquets and other-
wist; be phonographed in advance and machined to
his audience.

bishop a house, “ kindly allowed him to pay two-
thirds of the rental of a house, and when his long-
talked-of residence went up they cheerfully per-
mitted the bishop to give a thousand or so to
defraying the cost and to become liable for the
remainder.”

The new temperance hotel on the Blue
Mountains is to have public dances every night.
“At halt-past nine the gong will announce the
dance with the order ‘ Ladies and gentlemen, take
your places,’ and to the soul-stirring and brilliant
music will the dance proceed.” So says the share-
prospectus. Yum-yuml And all on coffee, too.
m * *
What the newspapers blandly describe as
an “ unusual occurrence ’ took place the other
day at Collingwood (Melb.). It was nothing
actually alarming only the street suddenly rose
with a shriek and threw itself up in the air, and a
confused mass of paving-stones, women, dogs,
mud, policemen, typhoid-germs, and pieces of
thoroughfare were flung into the atmosphere.
When tiiey all came down again and sorted them-
selves out it was ascertained that an accumulation
of sewer-gas or something had burst, and that
Melbourne was apparently training to go into
business as aji active volcano. It wasn't anything
actually alarming; of course, it was only an
unusual occurrence; and there was an odour
attached to it which is said to have caused a cer-
tain amount of remark—partly because it was also
more or less unusual and partly because it was
strong enough to make an elephant weep and wish
that he were dead.
* *
The tale about Jessie Brown and “Dinna
ye hear the Slogan ?” at the Relief of Lucknow,
was invented by a Parisian lady who wrote sensa-
tional news-letters, and it was copied into the
Times, and other English papers. All the people
who were in Lucknow agree that it is a complete
fabrication, and so goes by the board another
Mock Pearl of History. But The Bulletin
wagers that it won’t disappear from the History
Books. Every schoolboy will know it in 19110.
* * *
A Queensland Divisional Board is accused
of having entered t‘4o-worth qQ banquet and
liquor us “ labour and material.” This is a very-
old inspiration in a new disguise. For instance,
“postages and sundries” in a balance-sheet is
generally a mild way of alluding to the sum which
vanished mysteriously along with the good young
clerk who taught Sunday-school and wore a smile
like a little moral hymn; and “ neces ary legal
expenses ” is a smothered hint to the effect that a
dread ully smart man in the office found out how
the accounts were faked, and had to be bought oil
with £250 cash and a saloon passage to England.
“Repairs to plant” signifies, as a rule, that the
horse the cashier backed didn’t win, and that that
officer is in a state approaching to distraction ; and
“Reserve Fund and Undivided Profits” is the
sum that the liquidator won’t be able to find any-
where when he comes to wind up the concern.
Also—and most important of all-“ Unpaid
(Capital ” is a mild way of referring to the amount
which the shareholders won’t pay on any account—
not if the whole board has to blaze in Tophet for.
want of it. The author of this paragraph devoted
his early life to commercial pursuits, and speaks
from experience. He generally appeared in the
balance-sheet himself as “ Horse-hire.”
* * *
Tu a northern police court last week a
witness said he wasn’t a detective but a member
of a private agency, and the examining lawyer
suggested that the difference between an alligator
and a crocodile wasn’t a matter of much public
interest. Still there is a difference. The alligator
draws a salarv and never finds out anything, while
the crocodile,' being a reptile on piece-work, has to
find out things that never happened to keep him-
self alive.
# * *
The custom of holding a dress-rehearsal
wedding upon the evening preceding the fatal day
has made its appearance in America. Everybody
engaged for a part in the real show is expected tc
turn up at the dress rehearsal. A stage-manager
is appointed, and he fixes the cues for the bride’s
emotion, and arranges all the business of family
embraces. He even makes the bridesmaids prac-
tise a beautiful, modest blush at that portion of
the solemn service where blushes ought to come in.
A mock parson reads the service and coaches up
the happy pair in a mellifluous rendering of “1
will.” It is a good idea altogether. Very soon
we shall get to dress-rehearsals for funerals, over
an imitation grave in the back-yard.

TO THE HEBE FAIR.
AFTER TOM HOOD.
To the Hebe fair
Sing a song of Marrson,
Never was a prayer
Said by finer parson ;
Barmaids never shall
Heed their critics snarly,
While they have a pal
In the sainted Charley.
Fill the bumper fair.
Drink to brother Marrson,
Shake your golden hair
At the gentle pardon.
Free from taint of sin
Is the powdered beauty :
Selling goes of gin
As a Christian duty.
Though a vulgar clod
Make rude observation, 1 -
She is serving God
In her own vocation.
Wrongly ’tis supposed
By the simple stranger,
Hebe is exposed
To a lot of danger.
You can bet your life
Ne’er a girl is chaster.
Till she is a wife
No man has embraced her.
Charley’s pretty friends
Doubtless him admire, as
He comes in and spends
Threepences in Shiraz;
Through the fumes of wine
(Which can never hurt you)
Hebe seems divine
In her maiden virtue.
Dames of higher ton,
Showing shoulders nudely,
Often carry on
Just a little lewdly :
Barmaids’ innocence
Is their special glory,
They on no pretence
Tell a spicy story.
Social purists and
Rechabites, we’re thinking,
At the bar should stand
Ruddy liquor drinking;
Nectar from above

Says the Medical Times and Register ;
Evidences of the dangers -of hypnotism accumulate more
rapidly than the proofs of its value. The capability of
being hypnotized is a misfortune ; the habit enhances the
instability of the nervous system, which it evidences The
control thus exerted by one mind over another can only
exceptionally be employed for good. More frequently, as
there i!5 {foo.l reason to believe, deception, rascality and
libertinismare apt here to find their opportunity,’ and
weakness its desired excuse for yielding. Out of this we
see in the future suits innumerable for violations of the
property and the person of patients; suits, whose dan-
gerous character the profession will not be lomr in
appreciating, if the practice of hypnotism becomes
general, and juries becomes convinced of its reality.
What sinister reputations have been built up by
ceitam Australian practitioners who have habitu-
ally resorted to mesmerism ! They are almost
classed with the chloroforming dentist, Bertrand '

Bright red gloves with grey gowns are the
very latest fashion. Modjeska remarks : “ Red
worn below the face deadens the complexion, worn
above the face, heightens the complexion. If
therefore, a w’onv-n wishes to subdue the colour in
the cheeks she should wear a red gown or plent'
of red ribbons about bet throat; on the other
hand, if she wishes to give her face a certain
touch of colour, let her wear a red hat or red
flowers in her hair.”

Mrs. Cruger, of San Francisco, says a local
paper, is not only the type accentuated of American
beauty, but has a reputation for the sharpest and
quickest wit, and a self-possession that never
deserts her. A very swagger man about town
called on this lady one afternoon in the past winter,
and sent up Ins card by the trim French maid,
who possessed the nameless charm of her race. In
the interval before Madame appeared, he employed
his time in making violent love to the aforesaid
maid, and when the lady of the house descended
in noiseless slippers, a sufficiently electrical picture
greeted her astonished eyes. Without a moment’s
hesitation, without a glance in the direction of the
offending visitor, she remarked : “Marie, how
often must I tell you to receive your friends in
the kitchen?” and turned upstairs, leaving the
humiliated man in a social eclipse from which ho
has not escaped.
Dear Bulletin, --You stated the other day
that Mount and Morris, who were sentenced by
Chief Justice Stawell to 15 years’ “transporta-
tion” for “ black-birding ■’ got clear off when
liberated on a writ of habeas corpus. You are not
strictly accurate. I was in court on the occasion
when Justices Barry and Fellows delivered judg-
ment. Mount and Morris, though in prison dress,
were at once turned out of the dock when the
judgment was concluded, but a big posse of police
weie lying perdu in Russell-street barracks, and
when the ex-convicts got out into Russell-street
they were surrounded, re-arrested, and lodged in
the Swanston-street lockup. They were brought
before the City Court next day “as convicts
illegally at large,” but the charge was absurd arid
they were then finally set free. Some years after-
wards they were heard of in America.—-J. L. F.

If your liver troubles you take Wolfe’s Schnapps.
At a N.S.W. aldermanic meeting a Mr.
Smith remarked to a very diligent reporter :
“Now don’t you go filling the Evening Howl
with stuff derogatory to me, or I’ll go for the lot
of you.” “ Mister Smith,” said that oracular
individual, while he kept pegging away at his
notes, “I am forbidden to say anything about
you, good, bad, or indifferent. Your name is
tabooed in the office of that great leader of public
opinion and enlightener of an intelligent com-
munity, and my private opinion is that the first
occasion your name will sully that stainless sheet
will be when it announces your untimely death at
the hands of justice.”
Mind you get Wolfe’s Schnapps.
Use Batho’s Baking Powder.

STRONG EVIDENCE.
(From the BLACKBURN TIMES.)
William Henry Holden, residing at 28 Whalley
Old-road, Blackburn, has evei' since 1872, at fre-
quent intervals, been subjected to indescribable
suffering, owing to terrible attacks of intense pain
and swelling in his feet. His agony at such times
almost drove him frantic. As is natural to sup-
pose, everything that was recommended for the
ailment, which was of an acute rheumatic nature-
was used, but either owing to the stubbornness of
the complaint or other causes, all the remedies
applied proved futile. A few weeks ago, Mr.
Holden, hearing how the life of Mr. William
Buchanan, one of the Cunard Steamship Com-
pany’s engineers, had been saved by St. Jacobs
oil, after he had been given up by the Liverpool
doctors to die and had been at times deprived of
his reason by suffering, resolved to induce his son
to try this oil, and procured a bottle from Jackson
and Co.’s drug store. To his utter amazement,
and that of all the members of his family, the
excruciating pain and terrible swelling left after
only a few applications of the remedy had been
made, and the young man is now free from his
sufferings, “The change brought about by this
bottle of St. Jacobs oil is simply wonderful,” re-
marked Mr. Holden; “and I gladly state that
it has done more for my son in a single minute
than all the remedies we had used before that
time had accomplished in fourteen years. This
may seem like a broad statement, but it is the
plain, simple truth ; and I gladly give my consent
to the publication of our experience with this
wonderful remedy.” It may be here stated that
leading newspapers of Manchester and Liverpool
have recently reported similar remarkable cures.
Among these is that of Mr. Jno. Tetlow, a well-
known resident of Oldham, who had long been so
seriously afflicted with rheumatism that he was
unable to walk. He was bent with agony. A
single bottle of St. Jacobs oil effected such a mar-
vellous change that he is not only free from pain,
but, although sixty years of age, he can walk as
well as ever. So delighted was he with his relief
that he personally sent a testimonial to the Great
Britain Branch of the proprietors, the Charles A,
Vogeler Co., 45 Farringdon-road, London, with
full permission to have such use made thereof as
they wished. The case of Mrs. Ann Watson, of
Upper Pitt-street, Liverpool, is another instance
of the incalculable value of this marvel of healing.
The lady writes that she had been entirely de-
prived of the use of her feet owing to rheumatic
troubles of long standing. Physicians could do
nothing for her, and all medicines which she used
were without avail. St. Jacobs oil was finally
used, with the same astonishing results as in the
instances above reported. She can walk as well
as ever, and is in perfect health. Facts like the
foregoing speak for themselves, and it must be
admitted by all that so valuable a discovery as
that herein referred to deserves to be made known
everywhere.
DEY AIN’T BUILT THAT WAY.
Wat-er-berry nice watch fo’ all de boys,
Said Gus, to his chum one day ;
De cheap Swiss trash wont stand like dis,
Kase, why ? Dey ain’t built that way.
I’m bettin’ a dime that it’ll keep time,
And I heerd a gemman say
Dat de springs won’t break like cheap Swiss trash
Kase, why ? Dey ain’t built dat way.
Took free dollars and ’arf to buy dis watch
Yes, dat’s what I had to pay !
Warn’t any odder style so good fo’ de money—
Kase, why ? Dey ain’t built dat way.
W’en I fust put it on wid a bran’ new chain
Yer can bet that I looked just gay ;
Dar hain’t any kind so nice for to wind—
Kase, why? Dey ain’t built dat way.
An’ I told yer, boys, that the gemman said
Dat dey make more an er thousan’ a day ;
Oh, dis watch is de boss, all odders back down
Kase, why ? Dey ain’t built that way.
