BRUCE SMITH.
THE REFEREE.
A subscription—which, though much-pushed,
up to date looks very sickly —was set on foot to raise a
monment to the departed sculler, Searle. And now it
appears that the professional glory - engineers who,
after taking part in the promotion of a “public funeral
to Searle,” allowed the colossal bill to be footed by the
dead lad’s poor old struggling father, have up to now
permitted the doctor’s bill (£200), for attendance on Searle
during his last illness, to remain unpaid. It is not likely
that the public will consent to whatever memorial-money
has hitherto been subscribed being spent in a gaudy
tombstone while father Searle is being writted by the
doctor who did his best to keep the son alive. This latest
development of the “sentiment” of Australian sport is a
gross scandal, and the moral of it should not be wasted on
the scullers who are now metaphorically chained-up in
public-house back-yards.
# * *
What does the “ glory,” the “ upholding of the
honour of Australia,” all amount to ? What is its net
result on the side of the sculler? If “successful,” the
oarsman usually becomes landlord of a pub., and, in the
long run, dies in a “kidney-fit,” amidst the curses and
jeers of the wives and widows of all the sots he has
filled up with bad gin. He has a gorgeous funeral,
attended by three or four hundred cabs, each containing
two men with enlarged livers, red noses, shiny black suits,
and huge gold watch-chains—a funeral as revoltingl.y
vulgar as the hideous “ illuminated testimonial ” pre-
sented to him by his parasites a month after his great
victory and exposed in some shop-window for the edifica-
tion of the tough who wears a red necktie bedizened with
a brass pin.
# #
Had Searle gone to America absolutely “on his
own hook,” as he was so often advised to do by The
Bulletin, he could, merely by accepting the best available
local offers of business, have-made an independence by
exhibition. Once again we give our advice, gratis , to
Stansbury, who will, very likely, be the next champion.
So soon as the title -is his, let him throw backers to the
dogs, go over to the States and wade in at exhibitions —
not championship races, for these, while he holds the title,
should only be rowed on the Parramatta —for all the title is
worth. It is hard to estimate what the popular champion,
Beach, might not have made, had he accepted the advice
we tendered him when in the zenith of his glory.
* # *
The Sydney shandy-gaffers—[Bulletin co,"j •
right]—still continue their juggling over the sculling
championship. They now refuse to consent to Kemp
being matched against O’Connor for the championship
unless the race is for at least £5OO aside. It was, they
observe, Hanlan, O’Connor’s countryman, who first in-
sisted on the championship battle-money being put up to
that amount, and O’Connor himself wanted to row for
£5OO when he first came here. As to the first contention,
the lovers of sport as sport (as distinguished from the
betting clique), who will in the long run arbitrate and
decide in such matters unless sculling is to stink as pro-
fessional pedestrianism has stunk, do not feel them-
selves bound by the actions of O'Connor’s slippery
countryman any more than they consider O’Connor
bound by the actions of his countryman, Courtney,
whose racing-shell was so mysteriously sawn. Nor do
they consider that interested persons of the sporting-pub-
lican calibre can possibly be constituted a court of appeal
in what is, after all, a semi-national matter. Two blacks
don’t make a white. O’Connor, perhaps, had more money
or stronger backers when he first came here, or he thought
when he first arrived that he had a softer thing than now
turns out to be the case. As to the offer of some unspeci-
fied sportsmen or, more probably, betting-man, that he
will put up £5OO or £lOOO on O’Connor’s behalf if the latter
will “show him a good enough mile on the river,” The
Bulletin takes it that O’Connor or any other champion-
ship aspirant should in the interest of fair sport refuse to be
thus entrapped into tacitly ratifying the vicious £5OO pre-
cedent manufactured by, and now quoted by, the sculling
shandy-gaffers aforesaid. Mr. Frank Punch’s resolve that
no larger stake than £2OO shall be put up for O’Connor in
any championship race is a most commendable one.
Mind you, The Bulletin has no stock in O’Connor, has
never communicated with him, and has never spoken to
or heard from him up to the present hour. It has no wish
to set him up as an angel or to have him regarded as one
swayed by motives in any way superior to those which
prompt Kemp or the shandy-gaffers to action. But in
this case it clearly sees that he is on the right side —that
he is fighting, whatever may be his motives, the battle of
every young healthy sculler who doesn’t wish to have to
go hat in hand to a grog-shop or a bookmaker’s office.
# * *
Kemp’s fair-sounding offer to row for “nix”
after refusing a match with O’Connor for £2OO a-side, re-
minds us of the Cheap Jack who says: “ S’elp me, I’d
rather give it to you ! ”
# * #
Says the S.M. Herald—“ Stansbury has equal
credit as a rower with O’Connor.” If the writer means—
his language is decidedly ill-chosen and ambiguous—that
Stansbury’s performances are equal in number and merit
to those of O’Connor, then his statement is misleading,
for while the former has scarcely won a race and never
defeated a man of note, O'Connor has defeated all the
best scullers in America.
# # *
The sporting cablegram fiend has attained hia
ne plus ultra of idiocy in Melbourne Age w r here he
announces that Kirkham has not a 1000 to 1 chance of
winning the Epsom Derby, but is likely to win the Ascot
Gold Cup (a fortnight later) and that Narellan is likely to
win the Csesarewich —which latter is a handicap run in
October. It is but common charity to suppose that “Our
Own Correspondent” is the office-boy, and that this bosh
is compiled on the premises. On what ground does the
Australian press base such a splendid belief in the staying
power of Mr. White’s horses which is to enable them
to win over 2J or 2t miles when they can’t hold their
own over miles ? “ Staying power ”is a relative term
merely. A brilliant six-furlong sprinter will tire before
covering a mile in the very best company, and yet donkey-
lick a lot of second-raters over two miles. The flying roarer,
Prince Charlie, could make no show in the Derby, but for
the Leger, a quarter of a mile longer race, he finished
second. The one fast horse in the field beat him, but the
others were not good enough to find out his weak spot.
Of Mr. White's pair it appears to us that Kirkham is a
fairly good horse, who has already made a decent show in
good company, and will win a race when he competes
against animals slower than, himself—animals on which
he can “ wait,” instead of being himself waited upon.
“ Mr. James White’s colt Narellan is suffering
from warbles."— (Cablegram.): —
Narellan has the warbles ! Austral weeps
By day, and wildly mutters as she sleeps—
“ The colt has warbles underneath his skin."
Rare visions of the creature romping in
For that historic race on Epsom Downs,
Amidst hosannas sung by dukes and clowns.
Forever blotted out! The horse may die
And ne’er once catch the judge’s heagle eye.
He has the warbles—not the worthy judge.
But our Narellan. Ere you answer “ Fudge !"
Reflect upon the stuff they cable out—
“ The Prince of Wales, he hasn’t got the gout,"
Or smaller items, e’en of less import,
“ The Queen has ordered mourning at the Court."
And if the specials send such facts as these 1
We fain would know when Kirkham wants to snfieze ;
No news too trivial can readers get . ■% t
Which bears upon the subject of a bet.
The Czar of Russia sprained his little toe—
Dolled-up Augusta died, and went below—
The King of Spain is ill, poor, puling kid 1
We never back these people for a “ quid.”
We heed them not, nor love their royal baubles, ■
But grieve to hear Narellan has the warbles.
# # #
Warwick’s (Q.) champion log-chopper, Dan
Nedan, the other day chopped through a log of dry hard-
wood 18 inches in diameter in ten and a-half minutes.
# # *
The ’Frisco Breeder and Sportsman says that
Joe and Barney Thompson, the Australian “books,” have
offered to take' £20,000 worth of stock, if the rest of the
needful can be raised, to build a track of the best possible
description somewhere near ’Frisco. The intention ia to
have a lawn for ladies’ promenade and all the luxuries of
a Caulfield or Randwick.
* # #
For the Birthday Cup at Randwick on Satur-
day, Insigna and Magnus had most of the game to them-
selves during (he running, as they kept passing and re-
passing each other until the critical moment came, when
{he unjockey-like game told its tale, and Eclipse, who had
been nursed whilst the two leaders had been knocked
about, came away and won by a length from Insignia.
.# # *
The win of Eclipse on Saturday naturally brings
to mind his illustrious namesake, who flourished some 120
years ago. Eclipse was a racer of the first order. On
one occasion, after winning the first heat of a race, his
owner, one O’Kelly, offered to “ place ” all the horses in
the next heat, and after taking up a big sum in bets, he
declared “Eclipse first, the rest nowhere.” And sure
enough Eclipse distanced his field. Eclipse was never
beaten, and in his short career of seventeen months on
the turf, netted his owner £25,000. He did not race until
five years old, and he sired no less than 364 winners, who
won more than £160,000, exclusive of cups and plates.
* * *
A fluffy-haired Sydney barmaid dreamed on
Friday night that a mouse-coloured horse won a big race.
She went out next day to Randwick with a “ friend,” saw
the lately-clipped Eclipse in the paddock and enquired
his name. They won a hundred on the Birthday Cup.
Lots of other people whose dreams were of bays, chestnuts
and greys also backed their visions. They all got rats.
* # *
At the Australasian championship meeting on
Sydney Association Ground, out of eleven open scratch
events, seven went to New Zealand’s representatives, viz. :
Mile and three-mile walks to M‘Kelvey, the first after a
great go with Bullock, of the Sydney Harriers, the latter
anyhow; the mile and three-mile races to Morrison ; the
long jump to Cuff, after a great struggle with Kellett(Q.);
the 120 yards hurdles to Lusk—who is said to be 8 yards
worse than Batger, the N.Z. crack—and the 100 yards to
Hempton. “ Billy ” Macpherson, downed by Hempton in
the short race, put him through easily in the 220 yards,
and secured N.S. Wales’ only other victory by winning the
quarter with eight yards to spare. For Queensland,
Ferguson was successful in the half-mile, and Kellett in
the high jump. Local records were beaten in the mile
walk, and the mile and half-mile races. The features of
the meeting were the walking of M’Kelvey and the run-
ning of Hempton, M‘Pherson, and Morrison. The latter
is one of the most symmetrical and best-styled amateurs
The Bulletin’s representative has ever seen.
* * *
Notwithstanding “Billy” M‘Bherson’s suc-
cesses on Saturday last at the N.S. W. Amateur Champion-
ship meeting, he was not in the best of “nick,” as he has
been under the doctor’s care for some time and has been
“THE VAGABOND”—IN A NEW ROLE.
" AS AN OLD SOLDIER, HAVING FOUGHT FOR THE DIVINE CAUSE OF LIBERTY.”
(From an early photograph.)
doing his work in an elastic stocking. In the 100 yards,
Hempton, the New Zealander, won by nearly two
yards from Macpherson in 10J seconds, but if the former
can run the distance in anything like the time he is
credited with, he could have lengthened the gap at will.
In the 220 yards, Macpherson had apparently little diffi-
culty in disposing of Hempton in 23 seconds, and the
quarter-mile fell to Macpherson in 53 l-10th seconds. The
quarter-mile, which is about the hardest race a man can
run, as it is hard going for the whole 440 yards, is con-
sidered Macpherson’s crack distance. At Newcastle he
did it in 50 3-sth seconds, and at Ashfield, starting off the
2-yards mark, he put it behind him in 49 4-sth seconds.
To the writer’s knowledge Macpherson was not fit to run,
and should he again meet Hempton in the 100 yards he
may reverse the verdict.

“Sid.” Ferry, who rode his father’s horse
Simpleton to victory in the City Handicap (S. A.) recently,
is a schoolboy of about 15 years, and one of the smartest
little riders that ever sat a saddle.

It was a noticfeable fact that the five horses who
fihished in front for the Birthday Cup at Randwick and
the first two for the Farewell Handicap, are geldings.

; Some men are easily satisfied. One tipster
picked three horses and named Eclipse as a “cockboat"

A Yankee horseman says there is as much in
the breeding of jockeys as in that of horses. He says the
cross should be sire Irish, dam English, grandam Jew. All
the dare-devil riders are Irish, the English are bull-dogs
for hanging on, and Providence favours the Jews.
• # *
Briggs and Lohtnann seem to be now of little
use against the Australians on a fast wicket, or else the
other bowlers for Surrey and Lancashire must be
unusually weak. With the ground in a bad state the
cracks of Shrewsbury’s last two visiting teams are about
as deadly as anybody going, and little Briggs, in par-
ticular, can make such averages as seven wickets for 33,
playing with Mr. Laverton’s Eleven. For every-day pur-
poses, however, Peel and Attewell are evidently worth
more than any three other English bowlers; which
reminds us that their averages out here, for
the Melbourne club’s imported “amateurs,” was
flrst-olas*. it is of them that the Australians
have most cause to be afraid, for they are less
likely to be “ collared ” on a run-getting day than are
Turner and Ferris. If the weather keep fine it is likely
that Attewell and Barnes will win the match for Notting-
ham, just as Peel, with his 12 wickets for 70 runs (an
astonishing performance against good batsmen on a good
pitch) pulled Yorkshire through.
* * *
Professor Miller walks disconsolate about Mel-
bourne looking for somebody to put the hammerlock
upon, but there is no taker. Lots have dropped into the
grave waiting for Miller to grow old, but then he won’t
drink grog. , •

Ernest Cavill, who goes to England to try for
the swimming championship, took a Sydney benefit the
other evening. The amount realised will just about pay
for the washing of his swimming-trunks. However, he
will be slobbered over when he wins.

“What has he left?” asked Crawley of
Sinuick, when Coldbug departed from this planet.
“Why, he has left the earth,” replied Sinniek;
“ What more could he leave ? ”
The women are now agitating to make
married men wear wedding-rings. A woman is
labelled in three ways when she marries! (1.) The
ring. (2.) Change of name, (3.) She is made
“ Mrs.” The man goes unbranded, scot-free. All
the waitresses in the restaurant may puzzle their
heads. They have infallible recipes for testing
married men, and yet distrust them.
N.S.W, Registrar-General Ward had under
him an enterprising officer or two who, without
his knowledge or consent, arranged on his account
a little overdraft of £BOOO or so at one of the Syd-
ney banks. The bank now wants the State to pay
the money, but the Much-Milked replies per
medium of Salomons, Q.C., “Oh, no, you must
sue Mr. Ward ‘ personally ’ for it.”
The wreck of an ancient mahogany-built
ship, silted up and almost buried in the sand, is
one of the curiosities of the Warrnambool (Vic.)
coast, and after many years of conjecture it is now
proposed to dig up the venerable relic, and shake
the accumulated dust of ages out of her bones, and
see where she came from, and when she arrived,
and how she got there. As far as can be made
out, she belongs to some unspeakably venerable
order of naval architecture, but her age as yet can
only be guessed at. Possibly she dates right back
to the days when able seaman Ham, and Shem,
uncertifloated first mate, and that primitive fore-
mast hand Japhet, got lost on a sea that hadn’t
any shore, and pensively fished for their own
drowned ancestors with a hard biscuit for bait,
and occasionally hooked on to something soft and
flabby which they took to be an extinct scoffer in-
flated to 16 times his natural size by swallowing
the Deluge. Possibly there may be found on
board a log written on granite with a pitchfork,
and in it a brief inscription stating that Jonah
was thrown overboard at 2 a.m., and was after-
wards heard using disrespectful language in a
north-easterly direction. Possibly it is a relic
of the days when Pharoah published his in-
coherent paragraph on the Egyptian atmosphere
on the famous occasion when there was a dry spell
among the pyramids. There are countless possi-
bilities about this aged remnant of the past, and
when the sea-weeds and the crabs and the old
dead oysters, and the decayed winkles are shovelled
off the deck, and the large-sized devil-fish is moved
out of the cabin, and the octopus is persuaded to
let go-his hold on the binnacle, and the sea-ser-
pent has judiciously backed out of the steerage,
there will be a chance to look for the captain’s
skeleton, and to find him still clutching in his
hand the remains of some primeval liquor which
was bottled by Moses, and the cork of which was
driven in hard by Joshua in the wilderness. And
still seated on the top of the bones there may be
found the framework of the captain’s parrot which
was asking him to scratch a poll in a language
long since extinct, at the moment when the vessel
went ashore and the whole show unexpectedly
collapsed. Very likely there was a high old revel
on board the ship, and consequently the hazy
myth at the helm was unable to see his
way, and ran himself and the rest of the
able-bodied spectres and ghouls aground on a
strange coast. Or possibly they had brought up
in a peaceful little bay, and dropped the half-ton
of granite that did duty for an anchor, and offered
up the first mate as a sacrifice to the gods, and
gone to sleep, and then during the night the
patchy-looking local cannibal came softly along in
a canoe, and silently spread his long, many-jointed
claws round ship and crew and cargo, and took
them all in according to the unassuming custom
of the country in those days. If there were any
ladies on board that obtrusive native probably took
them ashore and persuaded them, by concussion
with a prickly herb or a piece of a tree, to settle
down in the land, and adapt themselves to the
manners of the inhabitants—which were few and
far between, and to conform to their customs
which were numerous and beastly. And on the
other hand the old vessel was probably only the Sara! i
Ann loaded with fertiliser, and went ashore partly
because the captain was drunk, and partly because
the mate was too ignorant to know whether he
was going straight or not, and mostly on account
of the insurance money, which was duly paid to
the owner—the .same being a pious and respectable
man who held the plate at the church-door on
Sunday, and created a general feeling of surprise
when he eloped with a Sunday-school teacher
after embezzling all the property of his aged
parents, and left his wife in a destitute condition
with eight small grass-orphans to fake care of.
And probably further investigation will reveal the
fact that he was a plain, red-headed object with
feet like two monuments, and a fat voice, and a
squint, and that the name on his portmanteau was
Thomas Jones.

Notice to Artistic and Literary Contributors
Strictly Original matter contributed to Tiik Bulletin
will be paid for. The Editor ivill he glad to receive and
consider contributions as tinder :
1. Original humorous or political matter, illustrated or
otherwise. (Drawings must be made in clean lines
of Indian ink on white paper or Dristol-board.)
2. Unpublished anecdotes of living celebrities, or people
who ought to be celebrated.
S. Humorous / terns (from eight to forty-eight lines pre-
ferred ).
Serious poems, of similar or greater length, especially
those suitable for pictorial illustration.
5. Short Australian or other stories, up to, say, 3000
words. About half that length, or even less, will
he preferred.
All Contributions must he Written Legibly, on One
Side Only of the paper, and folded, not rolled. The
name and address of the writer must appear, in all
cases, on the MS. itself, and not merely on an accompany-
ing letter or paper. The Proprietors do not hold them-
selves responsible for Dejected Contributions; but, if
accompanied by a stamped and addressed envelope or
wrapper, every effort ivill he made to return them.
The Editor does not undertake to enter into any
correspondence respecting rejected Manuscripts.

Shipwreck and Science,
A Recent South Australian Occurrence.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Once upon a time a maiden, tender chick of forty-
three,
Wandered, full of meditation, by the surges of the
sea,
And her thoughts were of the future, of the golden
time to be,
When a gentleman of fortune would seek her for
his bride,
And she’d encourage him with “Boh! you
couldn’t if you tried ;”
When the seasons, lubricated with the oil of peace,
would glide
Like the fishes in the ocean, or the swallows in
the air,
And her own would twine his fingers in the tresses
of her hair,
And would dedicate his balderdash “to Geraldine
the Fair.”
Such the gems of thought that softly filtered
through her giddy brain
As she paced in dreamy silence only broken by the
main
Rolling shoreward waves that murmured with a
sorrowful refrain.
Then she smiled a smile of triumph as she thought
how he would kneel
And reverence his birdie, and she almost seemed to
feel
His lips on hers—when suddenly she squealed a
squeamish squeal.
For, standing straight before her, gazing out upon
the sea,
She saw a naan like Adam ere he climbed the
apple-tree—
In other words, his “costume” showed a lack of
modestee.
Then she turned, and, with her fingers widely
straggled o’er her face,
She scudded for existence, and she never slackened
pace
Till she found a mounted trooped whom she
pointed to the place
Where the awful thing had happened, and she
blushed a pretty pink,
And looked so shocked and bashful that the man
began to think
That perhaps the ancient lady had been looking
on the drink.
But he followed her directions and he found, the
story goes,
The gentleman whose hobby was a total lack of
clothes,
Who, glad to find an auditor, spun out his thread
of woes.
“ Alight !” he cried, “ Sir Trooper, and be seated
on this rock,
And while the waves in hollow caves the merry
mermaids mock,
The awful secrets hidden in my breast will I
unlock. ”
“ Speak on,” replied the trooper, “ unsophisticated
bloke, ”
As he filled his pipe with “ nigger ” and prepared
to have a smoke,
“Oh, speak ! eccentric Johnnie; tell your story ”
—and he spoke ;
“I was born in Madagascar, where the blossom'
laden breeze
Meanders with a monotone of music in the trees,
And the sun with golden kisses woos the surf of
summer seas ;
“ Where the water, leaping downward, whispers
soft its silver word,
And is answered from the thicket by the singing
of a bird ;
Wh- re the sound of strife and struggle among men
is never heard,
“For they always live in friendship, and if mat-
ters go amiss
Their code of morals teaches them to ‘ make it up
and kiss,’
But at an early age I left this brotherhood of
bliss.
“I had seen but seven summers when I went and
took a berth
Upon the Murra-murra, and my soul was full of
mirth ;
Then I knew not that existence was of very little
worth,
“ And my spirit leaped within mo, as upon a
pleasure trip
We sailed from Madagascar in our jewel of a ship;
But between the jug and gullet there is frequently
a slip;
“ And we never found the pleasure that we left
our homes to seek,
For the passengers were helpless for what might
have been a week,
And had started to recover when the vessel sprang
a leak;
“ And scarcely had we stopped it, when the first
lieutenant swore
He could hear the faint re-echo of the breakers’
distant roar,
And we verified his statement when the vessel ran
ashore
“ On an island uninhabited by man or bird or
beast !
As there wasn’t much variety of grub, we had to
feast
On crabs and beche de mer, and didn’t like ’em in
the least.
“ After fifteen years of waiting, we at last began
to bo
As weary as the Jews who crossed the sanguinary
sea
And had manna for their hreakfeast and their
dinner and their tea.
“ So I called upon the sailors to assist me in a
plan,
By which we might evacuate the island to a man
(For the only thing that hitherto we’d done had
been to scan
“ The broad horizon daily, bat to our unceasing
grief
It brought no hope); they helped me in a manner
past belief,
For each man sadly sacrificed his pocket-handker-
chief ;
“ And I sewed them all together very carefully,
and soon
I placed before the mariners a motley-worked
hallo n,
And the captain, sorely sobbing, said ‘Napoleon
George Goroon,
“ I am lost in admiration ! O, my comrade, let us
try
If a blazing fire will lighten it enough to make it
fly' ;
So he sat with me and slowly wo ascended to the
sky.
“ Then we drifted to the northward, and for two-
and-twenty days
We sailed in thirsty loneliness beneath the baking
blaze
Of Sol, and on the twenty-third Australia met
our g izo;
“ And we breathed our thanks to heaven, when
our frail asylum chose
To sink towards the ocean, so I dropped a suit of
clothes
The only suit I had aboard— and then again she
rose.
“ I was dizzy witli excitement, all the earth seemed
turning round,
And, as we reached the shore, I fell and bumped
upon the ground,
While the good balloon, made lighter by my exit,
gave a bound
“And vanished on its journey with the captain.
That is all ;
Unless you’d like to see conclusive witness of my
fall
In the bruises on my—system, though they’re
really very small,
“Which is curious.” The trooper rose and pub
his pipe away,
And said he’d like to hear him spin his yarn
another day,
So he took him to the township and invited him
to stay;
And he stayed.
J. LE GAY BRERETON.

English Gossip.*
[FROM OUR OWN CORRESPONDEN.]
London, April 21.
Her Majesty’s health is causing consider-
able anxiety in Court circles. It has long been
an open secret that her feet present an abnormal
appearance on those rare occasions upon which
she holds levies. When any members of the Royal
family have affectionately enquired as to her
health, and especially when any delicate allusion
has been made in reference to the royal pedal
extremities, Her Majesty has exhibited an amount
of temper which, in one less exalted, might be
termed positively undignified. The Court physr
cians have maintained strict secrecy as to the
nature of the ailment from which the Queen is
suffering, and much curiosity is naturally aroused
thereby. However, after much time and research
I have succeeded in probing the mystery. Having
learned that Her Majesty’s boots are specially
made for her by Messrs. Rabbits, I called in at
that enterprising firm’s establishment, in Newing-
ton Causeway. After purchasing a pair of their
fourteen-and-sixpenny elastic sides, I made friends
with the assistant who readily fell in with my
suggestion as to refreshment at the Elephant and
Castle across the road. There, after four ‘‘ threes ’
of Irish hot, I discovered the secret. Her Majesty
has corns and takes number-seven boots,
# # #
Apropos to this, I may mention that I
since found out the real cause of the late painful
estrangement between Her Majesty and Prince
Henry of Battenberg. The Prince, as is well-
known, has a habit of prowling about the royal
residences, especially in the direction of the
domestics’ apartments. Some few months ago,
whilst peering into one of the top attics in
Buckingham Palace, he observed Her Majesty
seated on the floor in the act of cutting her corns
with one of his razors. Hot words ensued, ending
in Prince Henry taking a hurried departure for
Constantinople. As you have since learnt by
cable, a reconciliation has been brought about;
but such was the ill-feeling existing between the
Prince and his royal mother-in-law, that it is
extremely doubtful whether he would have re-
turned so soon, had not his pocket-money got low
and the Sultan declined to cash any more of his
I O IPs.
* # %
The Prince of Wales has had a really
trying week. Owing to the Queen’s affliction, of
which I have already spoken, a heap of extra
duties has devolved upon him. On Monday he
had to attend the Epsom Spring Meeting, wind-
ing up with a visit to the Gaiety in the evening-
Tuesday was spent witnessing the Waterloo Cup>
and Wednesday saw him at the pigeon-match at
Hurlingham. He was again at Epsom on Thurs"
day and Friday, and also honoured the Trocadero
Music Hall with his presence on the latter evening.
On Saturday he was at the meet of the Four-in-
hand Club in Hyde Park, and acted as chairman
at the banquet given in the evening at St. James’
Hall in aid of the Distressed German Princes’
Relief Fund. In spite of his many arduous duties,
however, it cannot be denied that His Royal
Highness still has a tendency towards inordinate
obesity ; indeed, should he continue to put on
flesh at the present alarming rate, the throne will
have to be enlarged to meet the requirements of
his King Hal-like proportions.
# # *
By the way, it may not be generally known
that the Duke of Edinburgh is possessed of a wit
of no mean order. I heard an excellent mot of his
the other day. It appears that the conversation
had turned upon the subject of repairs that are
necessary at the various royal residences. The
Duke, carefully watching his opportunity, re-
marked that “whilst he was at Malta, his residence
was sadly in need of some Jfafte-rations and re.
fiairs.” With the single exception of Prince
Henry of Battenberg, all present were convulsed.
Her Royal Highness, Princess Beatrice, translated
the mot into German for Prince Henry’s edifica-
tion, but up to the time of the closing of the mail
it does not appear to have dawned upon him.
# * *
I am constantly knocking against pro.
minent colonial visitors. En passant, one can
scarcely walk ten yards without noticing a “pro-
minent Australian.” I regret to say that some of
them are inclined to be cool towards their older
acquaintances. For instance, I passed Graham
Berry a few days ago in Piccadilly, and although I
raised my hat, he regarded me with a stony gaze,
and did not acknowledge me in any way. This
display of bad taste on his part may have been
due to the fact that he was sandwiched at the
time between Viscount Dunlo and the Archbishop
of York, but it has nevertheless wounded me con-
siderably. By the way, I came across Henniker-
Heaton the other day. It was near Sb Martin’s-
le-Grand, and he was leaning against a pillar-
postbox, apparently in deep study. I asked him
the cause of his abstraction, and he told me he
was preparing a peroration to a speech he was
going to deliver in the House of Commons that
night. “ How would this do?” said he : “ After
these unanswerable argument t in favour of free
postage to the colonies I trust that the right
honourable gentleman the Postmaster-General's
ear Raikcs.” I told the gifted member for Canter-
bury that it was undoubtedly good, but that h 0
stood a good chance of being suspended for a week
for.it.
# * *
There is not much moving in theatrical
circ l es just now. Rumours were circulated the
other day that Irving and Ellen Terry had
decided on an Australian tour in which they
would take in, besides the capital cities, Mogo,
Condobolin, Wog Wog, and the Jenolan Caves.
However, I was speaking to Irving yesterday
whilst wo were in the Gaiety bar having a cold
sausage together. Ho denied the truth of the
rumour. Although he had a strong desire to visit
the colonies, where he was rejoiced to hear that
his dear old friend Toole was meeting with such
an enthusiastic reception, he did not see his way
to accomplish a tour. He thought it possible that
the inhabitants of the back-blocks districts were
not yet educated up to the mannerisms requisite
for the proper enactment of the rOlcs of Macbeth
and Shylock.
# * *
I was present last night at one of those
most enjoyable gatherings at the Garrick Club. I
sat next to Irving, and I never recollect seeing the
great tragedian unbend as he did on this occasion.
After emptying his soup into Beerbohm-Tree’s
tail-pocket, he dexterously slipped a dish of curry
and rice on Bancroft’s chair whilst the latter had
risen to address the company. Of course the
result may be imagined when Bancroft re-
sumed Ids seat. It says much for the bon-
homie which exists among the profession that
Bancroft thoroughly appreciated the humour of
the thing. It was Tully half-past three before the
gathering broke up, and Irving and I drove home
in a hansom. I may mention that lam occupying
an attic apartment in the eminent tragedian’s
residence, and could, if time permitted, relate
many more amusing anecdotes showing the mar-
vellous versatility o f this most remarkable of
actor-managers.
MONKBARNS.
ò
An Appeal.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Where’er you are, on earth or laid below it,
A shadow or a substance, dead or quick,
Hereby The Bulletin’s official poet
Begs you to be discovered, Jacob Kick !
This is the sum and substance of his pleadings
That you no longer under mysteries hide
If living, speak, and stop the whole proceedings,
If dead, appear, and hint at how you died.
Ho, who from week to week writes fluent verse on
All themes, impelled by hunger and by thirst,
Implores you (and as handling the third person
Is irksome, will address you in the first-
ire, then, a bard who on Olympian nectar
Should feed, but scarce can win the crudest hash,
And flattest liquors going) lost prospector,
To tell us somehow if by blow or gash
Of murderous hand you fell. We hate to pen it,
But by your present reticence you are
Unwittingly enriching Alfred Bennett
And selling bulky issues of the Statu
You’ve dug for gold, a butt for Fortune’s cap s,
Witli many a “ downstroke of the gleaming
pick
You’ve found it lately —for the evening papers
If never for yourself, poor .1 acob Kick.
You’ve filled men’s minds with very dark sus
picions
Of crime, no doubt, without intending it,
Till ragged boys dispose of late editions
Containing latest rumours about Schmidt.
Now ’tis a tale of trunk decapitated,
Found mouldering in the bush near .lillihg-'
Where once he camped ; and now it is relatejj
How, when he’dwelt at Rush worth long ag\ iy s
The publi; 1 ioke;l with most “ extreme disfavour
Upon his villainously ugly Dee ” ;
Now there are stories of some queer behaviour-
Of his at Wagga, or some other phic '•
Anon a wire describing ahh-aehe l relic
Of something dead that once had been alive ;
Then more from Tmhbarnmba and .lingellic
And everywhere; and then some four or live
New theories of where the monster did it,
Of how he lured you to a spot remote,
Hewed off your hapless head, and safely hid it,
Then started to make free with TayloKs throat!
Till we, with the fastidiousness of genius,
Grown tired of all this whisp ring chill and
strange,
Of hackings done in caves and horrors heinous,
Distinctly are desirous of a change.
You should he cognisant of all the trouble •*
Your absence causes, wheresoe’er you are.
The Evening News (with circulation double
All other papers) gives you many a par.
And far inland each Archimedean gasper
For life that thunders against local wrongs
Has dwelt upon the problem of Wee Jasper
And sold by ones and twos to shuddering
throngs.
But, feeling sure The Bulletin will reach you
Where you sojourn, we tell you we are sick
Of this suspense, and earnestly beseech you
To make some revelation, Jacob Kick.
You’ve precedents for any line of action
Whate’er your sta'e. How many have been
mourned
As dead, who, to the mourners’ satisfaction, ' '
Have very unexpectedly returned.
How often, too, blind Justice has been guided
By unsubstantial bands ! Why, hosts and host?
Of fell assassins’ fates have been decided
And retribution brought to them by gh'
If you’ve been slain—we speak as a well-wisher
To British justice—now’s the very nick
Of time to emulate the ghost of Fisher t
And show where you lie buried, Jacob mk !
